Sanusi's Preaching and Constant Dhikr
Describes Sanusi's preaching style and constant remembrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi centered his preaching on fear of Allah and remembrance of Him, tailoring his words to each listener.

Core Idea
Sanusi's speech was a living remembrance of Allah.

This lesson opens a window into Sanusi's mission: turning people toward God through fear and hope. His constant dhikr and personalized exhortation exemplify a teacher whose every word was worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the rhythm and key terms like 'khawf' and 'dhikr'.
The passage is short but dense in meaning.
2
Identify the core elements
The passage mentions two main aspects: his focus on fear of God and the afterlife, and his constant movement of lips in dhikr. Also note his ability to adapt his preaching to each individual.
3
Reflect on personal application
Ask yourself: How can one emulate Sanusi's mindfulness? What does it mean to 'preach according to what suits the state' of the listener?
This lesson is about character, not doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان جل وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱) ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح ، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض . . بكى وقال : لا إله إلا الله ، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟!(۲) وكان يقول : ( ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه ؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض. وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً .. تغيَّرَ ، وقال لضاربها : ارفق يا مبارك ! وكان ينهى مؤدبي الكتاب عن ضرب الصبيان ، ويقول : ( الله تعالى مئة رحمة ، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتّسم برحمة جميع الخلق وأشفق عليهم) (۳) وزاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه ، فطلب منه السماح ، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير ، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألم جدّاً وقال : فقدت الدنيا بفقده وكان يتصدق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة ، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ويقول : ( من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء ((۱) . وكان يؤثر الخلوات ، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار ، ويقول : این سكانها ؟ وكيف يتنعمون ؟ وكان يقول : ( كم من ضاحك مع الناس وقلبه يبكي خوف ربه ، فهذا شأن العارفين ((۲) وحاله مع الدنيا حال المسجون ، فقل نومه وطال صومه ، فكان ينام أول الليل ويحيي سائره ، مع التزام الصوم عاماً إن هو رجع إلى النوم متى استيقظ ، وهذا ما قد أثر في وجهه وصدق فيه ما قيل : ( باطنه حقائق التوحيد ، وظاهره زهد وتجريد ، وكلامه هداية لكل مريد ((٣) وهو مع هذا كله شديد الحياء ، حتى إنه كان يكتب الشفاعات للسلاطين حياءً ممن يسأله ذلك ، فلامه على ذلك أخوه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي يوماً ، فقال له الإمام : والله يا أخي ؛ يمنعني منه غلبة الحياء ، ولا أقدر أن أقول : لا أكتب ، إذا كان الحياء يدخل صاحبه النار فأنا أدخلها (٤)
His main preaching to people was about fear of the majestic Lord and watching Him, and mentioning the states of the afterlife, and along with this he would preach to everyone according to what suited their state, and rarely would you see him except that he was moving his lips in remembrance of God Almighty. And among the forms of his compassion and fear of his Lord, Glorious is He: that a wolf passed by him being chased by a hunter and his dogs, so they caught it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it thrown on the ground, he wept and said: 'There is no god but Allah, where is the soul that was flowing in it?' And he used to say: 'A person should walk gently and look ahead so that he does not kill an animal on the ground. And if he saw someone hitting an animal violently, he would become upset and say to the beater: "Be gentle, O blessed one!"' And he forbade the teachers of children from beating the boys, and said: 'God Almighty has one hundred mercies, there is no hope in them except for one who is characterized by mercy for all of creation and has compassion on them.' And some scholars visited him during his terminal illness, and he had wronged him, so he asked him for forgiveness, so he forgave him and prayed for good for him. And when the Imam died, this scholar wept over him and was very grieved and said: 'The world has lost him.' And he used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he said: 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity, especially during high prices.' And he preferred seclusion, and visited ruined places for reflection, and said: 'Where are its inhabitants? And how do they enjoy themselves?' And he said: 'How many a laugher among people while his heart weeps out of fear of his Lord, that is the state of the knowers (al-ʻārifīn).' And his state with this world was the state of a prisoner, so his sleep was little and his fasting was long, so he would sleep at the beginning of the night and stay awake the rest of it, with the commitment to fast for a year if he returned to sleep whenever he woke up, and this had affected his face. And what was said about him was true: 'His interior is the realities of tawḥīd, his exterior is asceticism and stripping away, and his speech is guidance for every seeker.' And with all this, he was extremely shy, to the point that he would write intercessions for the sultans out of shyness from the one who asked him for that. So his maternal brother, Shaykh ʻAlī al-Tālūtī, once blamed him for that, and the Imam said to him: 'By God, O my brother, overpowering shyness prevents me from it, and I cannot say "I will not write". If shyness enters its companion into the Fire, then I will enter it.'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam Sanusi’s character and piety. It is not a doctrinal argument but a description of his personal virtues. The text proceeds by narrating several aspects of his life: 1) His preaching: He focused on fear of God and the afterlife, tailoring his exhortation to each person, and was constantly engaged in dhikr. 2) His compassion: He wept over a slaughtered wolf, lamenting the loss of its soul, and advised walking gently to avoid harming animals, rebuking those who beat animals. 3) His care for children: He forbade teachers from beating children, stating that God’s mercy is only for those who show mercy to all creatures. 4) His forgiveness: A scholar who had wronged him visited him during his final illness; Sanusi forgave him and prayed for him, and the scholar later grieved deeply at his death. 5) His charity: He gave charity especially during hardship, promising Paradise for those who give charity during high prices. 6) His asceticism: He preferred solitude, visited ruins for reflection, and wept inwardly while outwardly appearing happy. He slept little and fasted much, and his appearance showed the effects of his asceticism. 7) His humility: Despite his piety, he was extremely shy, even writing intercessions for rulers out of shyness. When his brother criticized this, he defended himself by saying that if shyness leads to hell, he would accept it rather than overcome his nature. The passage does not contain any theological argument but presents a model of Islamic piety combining fear, mercy, asceticism, and humility.

Key Terms
ذكر الله
الخوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was Sanusi rarely seen without doing?
A) Eating
B) Moving his lips in remembrance of Allah
C) Sleeping
D) Writing

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's life was a continuous act of dhikr, and his preaching was a mirror of his inner state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that Sanusi was rarely seen without moving his lips in dhikr. When was the last time you went an entire hour without consciously remembering Allah? What would it take to make dhikr as natural as breathing in your daily routine? Commit to one trigger today (e.g., every time you check your phone, say 'Subhanallah').
?
Sanusi tailored his preaching to each person's unique state. Think of someone close to you who is struggling spiritually. Are you offering them generic advice, or do you truly listen to their state before speaking? Will you this week intentionally seek to understand where they are before saying a word?
?
The lesson says Sanusi's main preaching was about fear of Allah and the afterlife. When you reflect on the afterlife, does it genuinely move you to change your actions today, or has it become a distant concept? What is one concrete step you can take this week to live more in awareness of the Hereafter?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi
beginner
5 min
Sanusi's Compassion for All Creatures
Narrates Sanusi's compassion toward animals and children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's compassion extended to animals and children, and how he linked mercy to divine mercy.

Core Idea
True piety is mercy to every living being.

Sanusi's reaction to a slaughtered wolf and his advice on walking gently reveal a heart overflowing with compassion. This lesson explores how he saw the soul in every creature and forbade cruelty.

Guided Path
1
Read the incident with the wolf
Read the Arabic account of how Sanusi wept over the slaughtered wolf. Notice his exclamation: 'Where is the soul that was flowing in it?'
This shows he saw even a wolf as possessing a valued soul.
2
Examine his advice on harming animals
He advised walking gently to avoid killing insects, and rebuked those who beat animals harshly.
He called the beater 'O blessed one' sarcastically or gently.
3
Contemplate his command to teachers
He forbade beating children and linked mercy to receiving Allah's hundred mercies.
This connects compassion to divine reward.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Islam allows cruelty to animals or harsh discipline of children.
Correction: Sanusi's example shows that true piety requires mercy to all creatures, including animals and children, and that divine mercy is conditional upon human mercy.

Checkpoint
What did Sanusi say when he saw the slaughtered wolf?
A) 'Be gentle, O blessed one!'
B) 'There is no god but Allah, where is the soul that was flowing in it?'
C) 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity.'
D) 'How many a laugher among people while his heart weeps.'

Key Takeaway
Compassion to all creatures is a sign of true faith and a key to divine mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see an animal suffering or being mistreated, does it move your heart to pity? If not, what does that say about your hope for Allah's mercy, which Sanusi says is tied to mercy for all creation?
?
Sanusi wept over a wolf—a creature many despise. Is there someone or something in your life you consider 'unworthy' of your compassion? Will you allow this lesson to soften your heart toward that being before the day ends?
?
If you are a parent, teacher, or in any position of authority, when was the last time you struck a child or caused pain out of frustration? How would you reconcile that action with Sanusi's command to be gentle, knowing that mercy defines one's hope for Allah's mercy?

Sanusi's Forgiveness, Charity, and Asceticism
Describes Sanusi's forgiveness, charity, and ascetic practices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi combined forgiveness, generosity, and ascetic withdrawal as expressions of his spiritual state.

Core Idea
Forgiveness and charity are the fruit of a heart detached from this world.

This lesson follows Sanusi's final illness, where he forgave a wrongdoer, and then explores his charitable habits and ascetic lifestyle. His words and actions reinforce a life oriented toward the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the forgiveness scene
Read the account of the scholar who visited him during illness. Notice how Sanusi forgave him despite the wrong, and the scholar's later grief.
This shows Sanusi's magnanimity.
2
Reflect on his charity
He gave charity and commanded his family to do so, especially in hard times. His saying links charity to love of Paradise.
The phrase 'especially during high prices' emphasizes extra virtue.
3
Examine his asceticism
He preferred seclusion, visited ruins for reflection, wept inwardly, and disciplined his sleep and fasting to the point of affecting his face.
His interior was filled with tawhid, his exterior with zuhd.

This passage is a biographical account of Imam Sanusi’s character and piety. It is not a doctrinal argument but a description of his personal virtues. The text proceeds by narrating several aspects of his life: 1) His preaching: He focused on fear of God and the afterlife, tailoring his exhortation to each person, and was constantly engaged in dhikr. 2) His compassion: He wept over a slaughtered wolf, lamenting the loss of its soul, and advised walking gently to avoid harming animals, rebuking those who beat animals. 3) His care for children: He forbade teachers from beating children, stating that God’s mercy is only for those who show mercy to all creatures. 4) His forgiveness: A scholar who had wronged him visited him during his final illness; Sanusi forgave him and prayed for him, and the scholar later grieved deeply at his death. 5) His charity: He gave charity especially during hardship, promising Paradise for those who give charity during high prices. 6) His asceticism: He preferred solitude, visited ruins for reflection, and wept inwardly while outwardly appearing happy. He slept little and fasted much, and his appearance showed the effects of his asceticism. 7) His humility: Despite his piety, he was extremely shy, even writing intercessions for rulers out of shyness. When his brother criticized this, he defended himself by saying that if shyness leads to hell, he would accept it rather than overcome his nature. The passage does not contain any theological argument but presents a model of Islamic piety combining fear, mercy, asceticism, and humility.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's life shows that forgiveness, charity, and asceticism are interconnected expressions of a heart focused on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi forgave someone who wronged him at the end of his life. Is there someone in your life you have not forgiven? What is the cost to your heart of holding onto that grudge, and when will you release it? This very day, take the first step to forgive.
?
He gave charity especially in times of hardship. When you face financial difficulty or scarcity, do you tighten your grip or open your hand? Examine your last month: did you give when it hurt? Commit now to a specific act of charity this week, even if small.
?
Sanusi's heart wept while he laughed with people. Do you ever laugh while your heart is grieving? Perhaps you hide your fear of Allah behind a cheerful face. Tonight, sit alone and ask: what is the state of your heart? Let the tears come if they must. Make a sincere du'a to be among the 'arifin.

Sanusi's Humility and Shyness
Describes Sanusi's extreme shyness and his defense of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's extreme shyness led him to write intercessions for rulers, and how he defended this trait as part of his nature.

Core Idea
Even a saint's shyness can be overwhelming, yet it remains a virtue when not sinful.

This final lesson on Sanusi's character highlights a surprising aspect: his overpowering shyness (haya) that made him comply with requests for intercession, even from rulers. His defense against criticism shows that shyness, though sometimes problematic, can be a sign of a gentle heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of his shyness
Read the passage about Sanusi writing intercessions for sultans out of shyness. Notice his brother's criticism and Sanusi's response.
Haya here is shyness/modesty, not necessarily bashfulness.
2
Analyze his defense
Sanusi says he is prevented by 'ghalabat al-haya' (overpowering shyness) and cannot refuse. He even says if shyness leads to hell, he would accept it.
This shows his sincerity and inability to overcome his nature.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider the balance between shyness and duty. Sanusi's actions were not sinful, but his brother saw a potential issue. This shows the complexity of human character.
The passage ends the biographical sketch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان جل وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱) ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح ، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض . . بكى وقال : لا إله إلا الله ، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟!(۲) وكان يقول : ( ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه ؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض. وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً .. تغيَّرَ ، وقال لضاربها : ارفق يا مبارك ! وكان ينهى مؤدبي الكتاب عن ضرب الصبيان ، ويقول : ( الله تعالى مئة رحمة ، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتّسم برحمة جميع الخلق وأشفق عليهم) (۳) وزاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه ، فطلب منه السماح ، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير ، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألم جدّاً وقال : فقدت الدنيا بفقده وكان يتصدق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة ، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ويقول : ( من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء ((۱) . وكان يؤثر الخلوات ، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار ، ويقول : این سكانها ؟ وكيف يتنعمون ؟ وكان يقول : ( كم من ضاحك مع الناس وقلبه يبكي خوف ربه ، فهذا شأن العارفين ((۲) وحاله مع الدنيا حال المسجون ، فقل نومه وطال صومه ، فكان ينام أول الليل ويحيي سائره ، مع التزام الصوم عاماً إن هو رجع إلى النوم متى استيقظ ، وهذا ما قد أثر في وجهه وصدق فيه ما قيل : ( باطنه حقائق التوحيد ، وظاهره زهد وتجريد ، وكلامه هداية لكل مريد ((٣) وهو مع هذا كله شديد الحياء ، حتى إنه كان يكتب الشفاعات للسلاطين حياءً ممن يسأله ذلك ، فلامه على ذلك أخوه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي يوماً ، فقال له الإمام : والله يا أخي ؛ يمنعني منه غلبة الحياء ، ولا أقدر أن أقول : لا أكتب ، إذا كان الحياء يدخل صاحبه النار فأنا أدخلها (٤)
His main preaching to people was about fear of the majestic Lord and watching Him, and mentioning the states of the afterlife, and along with this he would preach to everyone according to what suited their state, and rarely would you see him except that he was moving his lips in remembrance of God Almighty. And among the forms of his compassion and fear of his Lord, Glorious is He: that a wolf passed by him being chased by a hunter and his dogs, so they caught it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it thrown on the ground, he wept and said: 'There is no god but Allah, where is the soul that was flowing in it?' And he used to say: 'A person should walk gently and look ahead so that he does not kill an animal on the ground. And if he saw someone hitting an animal violently, he would become upset and say to the beater: "Be gentle, O blessed one!"' And he forbade the teachers of children from beating the boys, and said: 'God Almighty has one hundred mercies, there is no hope in them except for one who is characterized by mercy for all of creation and has compassion on them.' And some scholars visited him during his terminal illness, and he had wronged him, so he asked him for forgiveness, so he forgave him and prayed for good for him. And when the Imam died, this scholar wept over him and was very grieved and said: 'The world has lost him.' And he used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he said: 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity, especially during high prices.' And he preferred seclusion, and visited ruined places for reflection, and said: 'Where are its inhabitants? And how do they enjoy themselves?' And he said: 'How many a laugher among people while his heart weeps out of fear of his Lord, that is the state of the knowers (al-ʻārifīn).' And his state with this world was the state of a prisoner, so his sleep was little and his fasting was long, so he would sleep at the beginning of the night and stay awake the rest of it, with the commitment to fast for a year if he returned to sleep whenever he woke up, and this had affected his face. And what was said about him was true: 'His interior is the realities of tawḥīd, his exterior is asceticism and stripping away, and his speech is guidance for every seeker.' And with all this, he was extremely shy, to the point that he would write intercessions for the sultans out of shyness from the one who asked him for that. So his maternal brother, Shaykh ʻAlī al-Tālūtī, once blamed him for that, and the Imam said to him: 'By God, O my brother, overpowering shyness prevents me from it, and I cannot say "I will not write". If shyness enters its companion into the Fire, then I will enter it.'

Imam al-Sanusi's Charity, Solitude, and Shyness
Biographical account of Imam al-Sanusi's personal traits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical traits of Imam al-Sanusi—his charity, solitude, asceticism, and extreme shyness—and how they reflect his spiritual state as a knower of Allah.

Core Idea
Imam al-Sanusi's life blended outward asceticism with inward shyness, rooted in deep fear of Allah.

This passage offers a rare personal portrait of Imam al-Sanusi, showing not just his scholarly stature but his everyday piety. Through anecdotes of his charity, visits to ruins, sleepless nights, and embarrassment before rulers, we see a man whose spiritual life was both intense and humble. Let these stories give you a sense of the man behind the matn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through before interpreting.
Notice the vivid anecdotes and personal tone.
2
Identify the three main traits
As you re-read, pick out the passages describing charity, solitude/reflection, and shyness. Note the specific actions and sayings attributed to the Imam.
Each trait is illustrated with a concrete example.
3
Reflect on the shyness anecdote
Pay attention to the story of the Imam writing intercessions for sultans out of shyness and his brother's rebuke. Ask yourself: What does this reveal about the Imam's character? Why is he willing to risk Hell?
This is the most striking part of the passage.
4
Consider the saying about his inner and outer state
Find the line: 'His inner being contains truths of tawḥīd, his outer is asceticism and detachment.' Reflect on how this sums up the Imam's life.
Connects the physical practices to his spiritual reality.
5
Connect traits to the concept of al-ʿārifīn
The Imam says of the knowers: 'How many a laugher among the people while his heart weeps from fear of his Lord.' See how his own life exemplifies this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يتصدق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ويقول: من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء. وكان يؤثر الخلوات، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار، ويقول: أين سكانها؟ وكيف يتنعمون؟ وكان يقول: كم من ضاحك مع الناس وقلبه يبكي خوف ربه، فهذا شأن العارفين. وحاله مع الدنيا حال المسجون، فقل نومه وطال صومه، فكان ينام أول الليل ويحيي سائره، مع التزام الصوم عاماً إن هو رجع إلى النوم متى استيقظ، وهذا ما قد أثر في وجهه وصدق فيه ما قيل: باطنه حقائق التوحيد، وظاهره زهد وتجريد، وكلامه هداية لكل مريد. وهو مع هذا كله شديد الحياء، حتى إنه كان يكتب الشفاعات للسلاطين حياءً ممن يسأله ذلك، فلامه على ذلك أخوه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي يوماً، فقال له الإمام: والله يا أخي؛ يمنعني منه غلبة الحياء، ولا أقدر أن أقول: لا أكتب، إذا كان الحياء يدخل صاحبه النار فأنا أدخلها.
He used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he would say: Whoever loves Paradise, let him increase charity, particularly during high prices. And he preferred solitude, and he would visit ruined places for reflection, and say: Where are its inhabitants? And how do they enjoy themselves? And he would say: How many a laugher among the people while his heart weeps from fear of his Lord—this is the state of the knowers (al-ʿārifīn). His state with the world was that of a prisoner: little was his sleep and long was his fast. He would sleep at the beginning of the night and enliven the rest of it, while committing to fasting for a whole year if he returned to sleep whenever he woke up. This showed on his face, and the saying about him proved true: His inner being contains truths of tawḥīd, his outer is asceticism and detachment, and his speech is guidance for every seeker. And with all this, he was extremely shy, to the extent that he would write intercessions for the sultans out of shyness toward whoever asked him that. His maternal brother, al-Shaykh ʿAlī al-Tālūtī, once rebuked him for that, and the imam said to him: By Allah, O my brother, overwhelming shyness prevents me from it, and I am not able to say 'I will not write'; if shyness drags its companion into the Fire, then I will enter it.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam al-Sanūsī's personal characteristics. It describes several key traits: his practice of charity, especially during hardship; his love of solitude and visiting ruined places for contemplation; his weeping from fear of God; his asceticism in sleep and fasting; the outward effect of his piety; and his extreme shyness, even with sultans. The passage concludes with an anecdote illustrating his shyness: he would write intercessions for rulers because he could not refuse out of shyness, and when rebuked by his brother, he stated that if shyness leads to Hell, he would still enter it—indicating his inability to overcome this trait despite knowing its potential sinfulness. The author presents these as praiseworthy qualities reflecting the imam's spiritual state.

Key Terms

خلوة
العارفين
حياء
شفاعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Imam al-Sanusi's statement about shyness leading to Hell means he considered shyness a sinful trait.
Correction: The Imam's statement is hyperbolic, expressing his inability to refuse due to his nature, not a belief that shyness is sinful. He acknowledges the potential consequence but shows his character.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi's life exemplifies the balance between inner knowledge and outer piety, with shyness as a defining, almost overwhelming, trait.

?

Imam al-Sanusi's Daily Worship and Routine
Description of Imam Sanusi's daily habits: fasting, eating, prayer times, and teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the key elements of Imam al-Sanusi's daily routine and his approach to worship and asceticism.

Core Idea
A saint's day: worship, teaching, and seclusion with minimal food and maximal devotion.

Imam al-Sanusi was not only a great theologian but also a model of disciplined worship. This passage gives us a vivid picture of his daily schedule, from his fasting like Prophet David to his hours of teaching and retreat. As you read, notice how his piety was combined with gentleness and normalcy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the description of his routine sink in.
2
Identify key habits
List his fasting practice, eating habits, social behavior, and prayer timings.
Notice his consistency and humility.
3
Trace his daily timeline
Break down his day from Fajr to Isha: what did he do after each prayer?
The structure of his day revolves around worship and teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحواله في يومه، وأثرة من أخلاقه رحمه الله تعالى يصوم يوماً بيوم صوم سيدنا داود على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام، ويفطر على يسير الطعام، ولا يبحث يوم فطره عمَّا يأكل، وربما بقي ثلاثة أيام أو أزيد لا يأكل ولا يشرب، إن أتي بطعام أكل، وإلا بقي كذلك، وربما سألوه بعد مضي جلّ النهار: هل مفطر هو؟ فيقول: لا مفطر ولا صائم، فيقال له: لِمَ لا تعلمنا بفطرك؟! فيبتسم. وربما مازح بعض أصحابه، فلا تجد أحسن منه حينئذ، ولا يرفع صوته، بل يعتدل فيه، ويصافح الناس ولا يمنع من قبل يده، ولا يلبس لباساً مخصوصاً يعرف به، بل ثوبه ما اعتاده الناس. كان يكره الكلام بعد الصبح والعصر، ويتراخي في صلاته بتكبيرة الإحرام بعد الإقامة، ولا يكبر إلا بعد حين، وكان بعد صلاة الصبح في مسجده يقرأ أوراده، ثم يباشر بإقراء العلم إلى وقت الفطور المعتاد، ثم يقف ساعة مع الناس على باب داره، ثم يدخل ويصلي الضحى قدر قراءة عشرة أحزاب، ثم يشتغل بالمطالعة نهاراً. وكان بعد الزوال يخرج لخلواته إلى الغروب، وأحياناً يصلي الظهر مع الناس ثم يجلس يصلي ويتنفل ويقرأ إلى العصر، ثم يشتغل بورده إلى الغروب، فإذا صلى المغرب صلى ست ركعات، وبقي في مسجده حتى يصلي العشاء، ويقرأ ما تيسر.
His daily states and some of his character traits—may Allah have mercy on him: He fasted day by day like the fast of our master David (upon our prophet and him be prayer and peace), and broke his fast with little food, and would not search on his day of breaking fast for what he would eat. He would perhaps remain three days or more without eating or drinking. If food was brought to him, he ate; otherwise, he remained as he was. And perhaps they would ask him after most of the day had passed: 'Are you breaking fast?' He would say: 'Neither breaking fast nor fasting.' So it would be said to him: 'Why do you not inform us of your breaking fast?' And he would smile. And he would perhaps joke with some of his companions, and you would find none better than him at that time, and he would not raise his voice, but rather moderate it. He would shake hands with people and would not prevent kissing his hand. He did not wear a specific garment by which he was known, but his garment was what people were accustomed to. He disliked speech after Fajr and ʿAṣr. He would delay in his prayer by the opening takbīr after the iqāma, and would not say the takbīr except after a while. After the Fajr prayer, he would read his litanies (awrād) in his mosque, then proceed to teaching knowledge until the customary breakfast time. Then he would stand for an hour with the people at the door of his house, then enter and pray Ḍuḥā the length of reading ten ḥizb, then occupy himself with reading (muṭālaʿa) throughout the day. After noon (zawāl), he would go out for his retreats (khalawāt) until sunset, and sometimes he would pray Ẓuhr with the people, then sit praying supererogatory prayers and reciting until ʿAṣr, then occupy himself with his litany (wird) until sunset. When he prayed Maghrib, he would pray six rakʿas, and remain in his mosque until he prayed ʿIshāʾ, and would recite whatever was easy.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of Imam al-Sanūsī's daily schedule and character habits. It begins with his fasting practice: he fasted every other day like the prophet David, ate little, and sometimes went three days or more without food or drink, eating only if food was offered. He would not announce his fast; when asked, he gave ambiguous answers. He was pleasant in joking, moderate in voice, allowed hand-kissing, and wore ordinary clothes. He disliked talking after Fajr and ʿAṣr. In his prayers, he would delay the opening takbīr after the iqāma. His daily routine: after Fajr he read his awrād (litanies), then taught until breakfast, then greeted people briefly, then prayed Ḍuḥā reciting ten ḥizb (sections of Quran), then studied (muṭālaʿa) for the rest of the day. After noon he went to his retreats until sunset; sometimes he prayed Ẓuhr with the congregation and then spent time in supererogatory prayer and recitation until ʿAṣr, then focused on his wird (prescribed litany) until sunset. After Maghrib he prayed six rakʿas and stayed in the mosque until ʿIshāʾ, reciting whatever was easy. The passage presents a picture of disciplined, ascetic life devoted to worship, teaching, and seclusion.

Key Terms

أوراد
مطالعة
خلوات
ورد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: His refusal to answer whether he was fasting was due to secrecy or deception.
Correction: It was likely to avoid showing off and to maintain humility; he smiled when asked.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Sanusi's life shows that deep knowledge and intense worship can coexist with humility and normal social interaction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that Imam al-Sanusi would sometimes go three days without food or drink by choice. When was the last time you voluntarily endured even a minor discomfort for the sake of worship? What is one small deprivation you can commit to today—not to impress others, but to test your own sincerity?
?
He spent an hour each day standing at his door greeting people, yet after Fajr and Asr he disliked speaking. Do you guard your tongue after prayer, or do you immediately fall into idle chatter? Will you hold yourself silent for just ten minutes after Fajr tomorrow, using that time to reflect on what you have read?
?
Despite his intense schedule of fasting, seclusion, and worship, he joked with companions and did not wear distinctive clothes. Which of your religious habits is most at risk of becoming a show—perhaps your prayer, your beard, or your hijab? What would it take for you to worship Allah as if no one is watching, even for one day?

Imam Sanusi's Character and Social Conduct
Describes Imam Sanusi's fasting, social interactions, and speech habits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Sanusi's character traits and social conduct as reflected in his fasting habits, interactions, and speech.

Core Idea
Imam Sanusi combined spiritual detachment with gentle social grace.

Imam Sanusi was not only a great scholar but also a person of remarkable character. This passage offers a vivid glimpse into his daily conduct—from his ascetic fasting to his humble interactions. By studying these details, we learn that Islamic spirituality is never separate from refined social behaviour.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fasting and food habits
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note how his fasting reflects detachment from worldly concerns.
He fasted like Prophet Dawud, every other day.
2
Observe his response when asked about fasting
Notice his cryptic answer 'Not breaking fast nor fasting' and his smile. This shows his gentleness and humor.
3
Identify his social manners
List the social behaviours mentioned: moderate voice, shaking hands, allowing hand-kissing, wearing ordinary clothes.
He did not seek special recognition.
4
Note his speech and prayer habits
Understand his dislike of speaking after dawn and afternoon prayers, and his habit of delaying the takbir.
This reflects his reverence and focus.
5
Summarize his character traits
Based on the passage, write down the key character traits: asceticism, humility, gentleness, humor, and piety.
These traits are all exemplified in his actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان أحواله في يومه، وأثرة من أخلاقه رحمه الله تعالى يصوم يوماً بيوم صوم سيدنا داود على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام ، ويفطر على يسير الطعام ، ولا يبحث يوم فطره عمَّا يأكل ، وربما بقي ثلاثة أيام أو أزيد لا يأكل ولا يشرب ، إن أُتِيَ بطعام أكل ، وإلا بقي كذلك ، وربما سألوه بعد مضي جلّ النهار : هل مفطر هو ؟ فيقول : لا مفطر ولا صائم ، فيقال له : لِمَ لا تعلمنا بفطرك ؟! فيبتسم وربما مازح بعض أصحابه ، فلا تجد أحسن منه حينئذ ، ولا يرفع صوته ، بل يعتدل فيه ، ويصافح الناس ولا يمنع من قبل يده ، ولا يلبس لباساً مخصوصاً يعرف به ، بل ثوبه ما اعتاده الناس كان يكره الكلام بعد الصبح والعصر ، ويتراخي في صلاته بتكبيرة الإحرام بعد الإقامة ، ولا يكبر إلا بعد حين ، وكان بعد صلاة الصبح في مسجده يقرأ أوراده ، ثم يباشر بإقراء العلم إلى وقت الفطور المعتاد ، ثم يقف ساعة مع الناس على باب داره ، ثم يدخل ويصلي الضحى قدر قراءة عشرة أحزاب ، ثم يشتغل بالمطالعة نهاراً . وكان بعد الزوال يخرج لخلواته إلى الغروب ، وأحياناً يصلي الظهر مع الناس ثم يجلس يصلي ويتنفل ويقرأ إلى العصر ، ثم يشتغل بورده إلى الغروب ، فإذا صلى المغرب صلى ست ركعات ، وبقي في مسجده حتى يصلي العشاء ، ويقرأ ما تيسر ثم يرجع إلى داره وينام ساعة ، ثم يشتغل بالنظر أو النسخ ساعة ، ثم يصلي إلى طلوع الفجر فيا لها من أحوال شريفة ، نال صاحبها المقامات المنيفة ! فأكرم به من عالم عامل ، ومنارة هدي تتلمحها أعين القُصَّاد ، وسالكي سبل الرشاد !
He was – may Allah have mercy on him – his states in his day and a trace of his character: he fasted day by day the fast of our master Dawud (upon our Prophet and upon him peace and blessings), and broke his fast on little food, and he did not seek on his day of breaking what he would eat; perhaps he remained three days or more not eating and not drinking. If food was brought to him, he ate; otherwise he remained like that. And perhaps they asked him after most of the day had passed: 'Are you breaking fast?' He would say: 'Not breaking fast nor fasting.' So it was said to him: 'Why do you not inform us of your breaking fast?' Then he would smile. And perhaps he joked with some of his companions, and you would find none better than him at that time, and he did not raise his voice, but was moderate in it. He shook hands with people and did not prevent kissing of his hand, nor did he wear a special garment by which he was known; rather his garment was what people were accustomed to. He disliked speaking after dawn and afternoon prayers. He delayed in his prayer by the takbirat al-ihram after the iqama, and he did not say the takbir except after a while. After the dawn prayer he would recite his litanies in his mosque, then he would begin teaching knowledge until the customary breakfast time. Then he would stand for an hour with people at the door of his house, then enter and pray the forenoon prayer (duha) to the extent of reciting ten ajza' (sections) of the Quran, then occupy himself with reading during the day. After noon, he would go out for his retreats until sunset. Sometimes he would pray the noon prayer with the people, then sit praying and performing voluntary prayers and reciting until the afternoon prayer, then occupy himself with his litany until sunset. When he prayed the sunset prayer, he would pray six rak'as and remain in his mosque until he prayed the evening prayer, and he would recite what was easy. Then he would return to his house and sleep for an hour, then occupy himself with contemplation or copying for an hour, then pray until dawn. What noble states are these! Their possessor attained lofty stations! How noble is he as a learned and practicing scholar, a beacon of guidance sought by the eyes of those who approach and those following the paths of rectitude!
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of Imam Sanusi's daily habits and character. It begins by detailing his fasting practice: he fasted every other day like Prophet Dawud and ate very little when breaking the fast, without concerning himself about the source of food. He sometimes went days without food or drink, eating only if offered. When asked if he was fasting, he would give a cryptic answer 'Not fasting nor fasting' and smile. The passage then describes his gentle social interactions: he joked affectionately, spoke moderately, shook hands, allowed his hand to be kissed, and wore ordinary clothes. His prayer habits include delayed takbirat al-ihram. His daily schedule is then outlined: after dawn prayer he recited litanies, taught until breakfast, then greeted people, prayed duha extensively, and studied. After noon he retreated until sunset, sometimes praying with the congregation and then performing extra prayers until afternoon, then litanies until sunset. After sunset prayer he prayed six rak'as, then remained in the mosque until evening prayer, reading Quran. At home he slept briefly, then engaged in contemplation or copying, then prayed until dawn. The passage concludes with praise of his noble states and high spiritual stations.

صوم داود
مفطر
صائم
تسبيح

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's character combined ascetic detachment with gentle, humble social interactions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your spiritual routine is disrupted, do you become anxious like someone who needs to plan every detail, or can you trust Allah and remain at peace like Imam Sanusi who didn't even seek food on his day of breaking fast? What small step can you take this week to detach from rigid habits and rely more on Allah?
?
Think of a time when someone questioned your religious practice. Did you respond with a defensive argument or with a gentle smile like Imam Sanusi? What is holding you back from responding with the same humility and humor?
?
Imam Sanusi wore ordinary clothes and did not advertise his piety. What outward symbols or behaviors do you rely on to signal your religiosity, and would you be willing to let them go for the sake of sincerity? What would that cost you—and what would it gain you?

Imam Sanusi's Daily Spiritual Regimen
Describes Imam Sanusi's daily schedule from dawn to night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Sanusi's detailed daily schedule of worship, teaching, and retreat.

Core Idea
Imam Sanusi structured his entire day around worship, teaching, and spiritual retreat.

The passage describes the daily routine of Imam Sanusi from dawn to night. It reveals how he balanced teaching, personal devotion, and community interaction. This schedule is a model for integrating knowledge and spirituality.

3

The Dream of Forgiveness: A Miracle Blended with Kalam
Narrates a dream vision of Sanusi's father, linking forgiveness to Sanusi's contemplation on the mountain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle of Imam Sanusi demonstrates the spiritual power of theological contemplation and the value of knowledge.

Core Idea
Theological reflection can benefit even the deceased.

This passage relates a remarkable dream vision that links Imam Sanusi's father's forgiveness to his son's contemplation on the mountain. It shows that the study of kalam (theology) is not merely academic but carries profound spiritual benefit. The author presents this as a miracle (karama) that underscores the sincerity of Imam Sanusi.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of karamat
The author begins by clarifying that miracles (karamat) are not what make Imam Sanusi great; his true stature lies in his knowledge and guidance. Read the opening lines to see how the author sets up the story.
Notice the phrase 'لا يعظم شأنه بحكاية كرامة له' – he is not glorified by relating a miracle.
2
Read the dream account
Read the specific miracle narrated by al-Shifshawni: a saint saw Sanusi's father in a dream and asked about his fate. The father attributes his forgiveness to his son's reflection on the mountain at the time of burial.
Pay attention to the father's words: 'بتفكر ولدي في الجبل ساعة دفني'.
3
Reflect on the link between contemplation and forgiveness
Imam Sanusi confirms he was reflecting on theological matters at that moment. Consider how this story ties the act of theological reflection (kalam) to divine mercy.
This illustrates the author's earlier point that some of his miracles are 'ممزوجةً بعلم الكلام' – blended with kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرف من كراماته مثل الإمام السنوسي لا يعظم شأنه بحكاية كرامة له ؛ إذ حسبنا ما أكرمه الله به حين بواه هذه المنزلة العلمية الرفيعة ، وأقامه عَلَمَ هدى لإرشاد الخلق ، واستنقذ بما أجراه الله على يراعه كثيراً من التائهين المتحيّرين ، ولكن حكاية ذلك من باب زيادة العناية والتعظيم ومن اللطيف أن ترى له بعض الكرامات ممزوجةً بعلم الكلام ! وهذا دال على صدق حال الإمام مع هذا العلم المقرب من الله تعالى ؛ فقد حكى العلامة الشفشاوني عن بعض الأولياء أنه رأى والد الشيخ السنوسي بعد موته في المنام ، فقال له : ما فعل الله بك ؟ فقال : غفر لي ، فقال : بم ؟ قال : بتفكر ولدي في الجبل ساعة دفني ! فلمَّا سُئل الإمام السنوسي عن ذلك قال : نعم ؛ كنت أتفكّر في (۱) انظر ( البستان » ( ص ٢٤٤ ) ، و ( نيل الابتهاج » ( ص ٥٧٠ ) .
A selection of his miracles. The likes of Imam al-Sanusi are not glorified by relating a miracle of his; for sufficient for us is what Allah honored him with when He gave him this lofty scholarly station, and made him a beacon of guidance for directing creation, and through what Allah made flow from his pen He rescued many of the lost and bewildered. But relating that is a matter of additional attention and glorification. And it is subtle that you see some of his miracles blended with kalam! And this indicates the truthfulness of the Imam's state with this science that brings one close to Allah Almighty. For the scholar al-Shifshawni related from some of the saints that he saw the father of Shaykh al-Sanusi after his death in a dream, and he said to him: 'What did Allah do with you?' He said: 'He forgave me.' He said: 'By what?' He said: 'By my son's reflection on the mountain at the hour of my burial.' When Imam al-Sanusi was asked about that, he said: 'Yes; I was reflecting...' (1) See al-Bustan (p. 244) and Nail al-Ibtihaj (p. 570).
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage introduces a selection of Imam Sanusi's miracles (karamat). The author first notes that Sanusi's true greatness lies not in narrated miracles but in his scholarly status and role as a guide. Miracles are mentioned only for additional attention and glorification. The passage then highlights a subtle point: some of his miracles are mixed with kalam (theology), indicating his sincerity in pursuing that science which brings closeness to Allah. A specific miracle is then related from al-Shifshawni: a saint saw Sanusi's father in a dream after his death. When asked what Allah did with him, the father said he was forgiven 'by my son's reflection on the mountain at the hour of my burial.' When asked about this, Imam Sanusi confirmed that he was indeed reflecting (on theological matters) at that time. The story ends with a footnote referencing sources. The passage thus demonstrates the spiritual power of theological contemplation and its benefit even after death.

عالم الكلام
تأمل
العلامة الشفشاوني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that karamat (miracles) are necessary proofs of sainthood or that they equal prophethood.
Correction: The author clarifies that Sanusi's true stature is in his knowledge and guidance, not miracles; the miracle is related only for additional attention and glorification.

C) Because of his son's reflection on the mountain at the hour of his burial.
D) Because he performed the pilgrimage.

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's reflection on theology at his father's burial became a means of forgiveness, showing the spiritual power of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die tomorrow, would your own reflection on theology—or lack thereof—be a means of forgiveness for your parents or children? What are you actually doing to ensure that your study of kalam becomes a source of benefit for those you love, both living and deceased?
?
Imagine you are at the grave of a loved one. What are you thinking about? Is it the same kind of deep theological contemplation that Sanusi had, or is it distracted? How can you train yourself to turn your thoughts at such moments into a source of mercy for the deceased?
?
The father was forgiven because his son was reflecting on kalam at the exact time of burial. Does your pursuit of Islamic knowledge have that kind of sincerity and presence? If not, what is missing, and what will you change today to make your study of aqeedah more than just academic exercise?

Imam Sanusi's Nightly Devotion: A Model of Discipline
Describes Imam Sanusi's nightly worship and study schedule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disciplined nightly routine of Imam Sanusi and how it exemplifies the ideal of a 'knowledgeable and practicing scholar' (عالم عامل).

Core Idea
Disciplined worship and study lead to lofty spiritual stations.

This lesson takes you into the nightly routine of Imam Sanusi, a great scholar and saint. His schedule—sleep, study, and prayer—reveals a life of balance and devotion. As you read, consider how his habits reflect the harmony between knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage silently
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the sequence of actions: return home, sleep, study/copy, pray until dawn.
Pay attention to the emotional exclamation at the end—it signals the author's admiration.
2
Identify the key activities
List the four actions mentioned: returning home, sleeping, scholarly work (nazar/naskh), and praying. Consider why each is placed where it is.
The hour of study/copying comes after rest, showing the importance of refreshment for learning.
3
Reflect on the praise
Examine the author's praise: 'What noble states these are! Their possessor has attained lofty stations!' and the title 'عالم عامل'. What does this tell us about the ideal scholar?
The combination of knowledge and practice is a central theme in Islamic scholarship.
4
Connect to your own life
Think about how you can incorporate balanced discipline into your own worship and study routines. What can you learn from Imam Sanusi's example?
Even a small, consistent routine can lead to great spiritual progress.

Imam Sanusi's nightly routine shows that balancing rest, study, and prayer is a path to spiritual excellence and practical scholarship.

A Miracle Involving Kalam Reflection
Relates a miracle where the Imam's kalam reflection benefits his deceased father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a saintly miracle (karama) can be intertwined with the science of kalam, as demonstrated by Imam Sanusi's reflection benefiting his deceased father.

Core Idea
A saint's reflection in kalam can benefit even the deceased.

This short anecdote from the life of Imam Sanusi shows that his practice of kalam reflection was not just an intellectual exercise but a source of spiritual power. The story tells how his father was forgiven after death due to the Imam’s contemplation on a mountain. It illustrates the extraordinary baraka of sincere engagement with kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the narrative flow naturally before analyzing it.
The passage contains a repeated phrase (duplication) which is likely a copyist error.

3

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The father said Allah forgave him because of his son's reflection (تفكر) in the mountain for an hour after his burial. When asked, Imam Sanusi confirmed he was reflecting on the mountain overlooking Tlemcen, contemplating its jewels and how Allah composed it with His power and wisdom. This shows that his kalam reflection was so powerful that it benefited his deceased father.

The Meat That Would Not Cook
Narrates a miracle where meat avoids fire because it accompanied the Imam's prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle (karama) manifested through Imam Sanusi's prayer, demonstrating the spiritual protection granted to those who pray behind him.

Core Idea
The prayer of a saint can extend divine protection even to objects present with him.

This passage narrates an extraordinary event from the life of Imam Sanusi. A man's meat refuses to cook despite prolonged exposure to fire. The reason, as the Imam explains, lies in the baraka (blessing) of the congregational prayer led by him. The story illustrates the concept of karama (miracle) in Islam and the profound spiritual station of awliya (saints).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the full story. Notice the details: the man's actions, the cooking attempts, and the Imam's response.
The story has a clear sequence of events.
2
Identify the miracle
The miracle is that the meat did not cook despite hours over a fire. The Imam attributes this to the fact that the meat was present during his prayer. Recognize that this is a karama (miracle) of a saint.
The Imam says he hopes that whoever prays behind him will not be harmed by fire.
3
Reflect on the spiritual lesson
What does this miracle tell us about Imam Sanusi's rank? Why did the Imam ask the man to keep it secret? Reflect on the humility and discretion of saints.
Saints often conceal their miracles to avoid fame.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كراماته : أنَّ رجلاً اشترى لحماً من السوق ، فسمع الإقامة في المسجد ، فدخل واللحم معه يحمله ، فخاف معه يحمله ، فخاف من طرحه فوات ركعة ، وكبر على هذه الحال ، فلما سلَّم وعاد إلى أهله طبخ أهله اللحم إلى صلاة العشاء فلم ينضج ، فأرادوا طرحه فإذا هو على حاله بدمه ، فقالوا : لعله لحم شارف ، فباتوا يوقدون عليه إلى الصبح ، فلم يتغيَّر عن حاله ! فتذكر الرجل ، فذهب إلى الإمام فأعلمه بذلك ، فقال له : يا بني ؛ أرجو الله تعالى أنَّ كل من صلى ورائي لا تعدو عليه النار ، (۱) ولعل هذا اللحم كان معك حين صليت معي ، ولكن اكتم ذلك (۱)
And among his miracles: that a man bought meat from the market, then heard the iqama in the mosque, so he entered while carrying the meat, and he feared that if he put it down he would miss a rak'a, so he said the takbir in that state. When he finished the prayer and returned to his family, his family cooked the meat until the evening prayer but it did not cook. They wanted to throw it away, but it remained in its state with its blood. They said: 'Perhaps it is old meat.' So they kept kindling fire on it until morning, but it did not change from its state. Then the man remembered, so he went to the Imam and informed him. He said to him: 'O my son, I hope from Allah that whoever prays behind me, fire will not overcome him. Perhaps this meat was with you when you prayed with me, but keep this secret.'
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage narrates another miracle of Imam Sanusi. It begins with a man buying meat from the market. He hears the iqama (call to commence prayer) and enters the mosque still carrying the meat. He fears that putting it down would cause him to miss a rak'a, so he says the takbir (pronounces 'Allahu Akbar') while still holding the meat. After the prayer, his family cooks the meat until the evening prayer, but it does not cook. They try leaving it on fire all night, but it remains unchanged, still with blood. The man then remembers the connection to the Imam and goes to inform him. The Imam explains that he hopes from Allah that whoever prays behind him will not be overcome by fire, and perhaps the meat was also protected because it was with him during the prayer. He instructs the man to keep this secret.

الإقامة
النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The fire was not hot enough.

Key Takeaway
The miracle of the meat that would not cook demonstrates the extraordinary baraka of Imam Sanusi's prayer and the protection it brings to those who follow him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Can you recall a recent situation where you spoke with certainty about something that is truly only in Allah's hands? How will you correct that today?

Tawassul and the Locked Door
A statement by Imam Sanusi encouraging tawassul and a supporting narrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of tawassul as practiced by Imam Sanusi and its efficacy as illustrated by the story of the locked door.

Core Idea
Tawassul through the righteous is a means to have one's needs fulfilled by Allah.

This lesson presents a direct teaching from Imam Sanusi on the practice of tawassul (seeking intercession) through him and the awliya. He not only taught it but a story also demonstrates its effectiveness. The passage shows the confidence he had in his Lord and the rank of the righteous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a general statement followed by a specific story.
2
Understand the principle
Identify Imam Sanusi's statement: 'Whoever has a need from Allah, let him seek intercession through us and put us forward.' Reflect on the meaning of tawassul and putting forward the righteous.
3
Reflect on the story
Consider the woman's situation: she tried everything, then turned to tawassul through the Shaykh. Notice the outcome: the door opened. Ask yourself what this teaches about tawassul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : أنه كان يقول ثقةً بمولاه : ( من كانت له إلى الله حاجة فليتوسل بنا وليقدمنا ) ، فرُوي أنَّ امرأة ضاع منها مفتاح بيتها ، فحاولت بكل حيلة فتحه فلم تفلح ، فتوسلت بالشيخ وبجاهه عند ربه ، ففتح الباب وانحل القفل(۲)
And among that: he used to say, trusting in his Lord: 'Whoever has a need from Allah, let him seek intercession (tawassul) through us and put us forward.' And it is narrated that a woman lost the key to her house, and she tried every trick to open it but failed, so she sought intercession through the Shaykh and his rank with his Lord, and the door opened and the lock came undone (2).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage shows that Imam Sanusi encouraged tawassul (seeking intercession) through him. He stated a general principle: anyone with a need should use him as a means and put him forward. Then a story is given: a woman lost her key and was unable to open her door; she performed tawassul through the Imam and his rank (جاه) with his Lord, and the door opened and the lock came undone. This illustrates the efficacy of tawassul.

Key Terms
توسل
جاه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawassul is a form of shirk or that it bypasses Allah and depends on the saint directly.
Correction: The passage shows the woman relied on the Imam's rank with Allah, not on the Imam as an independent power. The opening of the door was by Allah's permission, through the intercession of the righteous. Tawassul is a legitimate means in Islam.

Checkpoint

B) She used a tool.
C) She sought intercession through the Shaykh.
D) She called a locksmith.

Key Takeaway
Tawassul through the righteous is a practice endorsed by Imam Sanusi and proven effective by the account of the locked door.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever faced a difficulty where you tried everything humanly possible and still failed? Did you turn to tawassul through the righteous, or did you rely solely on your own efforts? What prevented you?
?
Is there a 'locked door' in your life right now - a problem you have not been able to solve? Will you sincerely seek tawassul through a righteous person, trusting that Allah can open it? Write down one specific need and the person you will make tawassul through.
?
Do you really trust that Allah responds to tawassul? Examine your heart: if you had a desperate need, would you be willing to humbly ask through the means of the righteous, or would pride stop you?

Sanusi’s Book Completion and His Vision of Hell
Two stories about Sanusi completing his book and his vision of Hell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Sanusi’s spiritual experiences illustrate the reality of karamat and the impact of witnessing the afterlife.

Core Idea
A saint's vision of Hell can transform the heart permanently.

These two anecdotes from the life of Imam Sanusi reveal the depth of his spiritual state. The first shows his care against the evil eye; the second unveils a profound vision that changed him. Together, they ground the believer's awe of the afterlife in lived experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdotes carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two separate incidents: the book completion and the vision.
The first incident involves a precaution against the evil eye.
2
Reflect on the precaution against evil eye
Why did Sanusi complete his book at age forty and seek testimony? This shows his humility and awareness of spiritual harms.
3
Consider the impact of the vision
Sanusi’s permanent sadness after seeing Hell shows the transformative power of such unveilings. It is not worldly grief but spiritual awe.
This connects to the reality of kashf (unveiling) for saints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكمل مؤلّفه أربعين سنة؛ لئلا يصاب بالعين، ويقول له: لا نظير له فيما أعلم، ودعا لمؤلفه. ومن ذلك: أنه رئي يوماً كثير الانقباض متغير اللون، فسئل عن ذلك، فأجاب بعد إلحاح وشرط كتم الأمر، فقال: أطلعني الله تعالى على رؤية جهنم وما فيها، نعوذ بالله منها، فمن حينئذ صرت أتغير وأحزن إلى الآن، فهذا سبب تغيري.
He completes his book at forty years, lest it be struck by the evil eye, and he says to him: there is no equal to it as far as I know, and he supplicated for its author. And among that: that one day he was seen very contracted, changed in color, and he was asked about that, so he answered after insistence and the condition of keeping the matter secret, and he said: Allah the Exalted informed me of the vision of Hell and what is in it, we seek refuge with Allah from it, and from that time I became changed and sad until now, so this is the reason for my change.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a narrative of two incidents from the life of Imam Sanusi. The first incident mentions that he completed a book at the age of forty and took precautions against the evil eye by having someone testify to its uniqueness and pray for its author. The second incident describes a period when Sanusi was observed to be in a state of contraction (inqibad) and his color changed. Upon persistent questioning, he revealed that Allah had shown him a vision of Hell, and this vision caused him ongoing sadness and change. The passage highlights the reality of karamat (miraculous states) for saints, as Sanusi experiences a direct unveiling of the afterlife. It also shows his humility and the weight of spiritual knowledge on his heart.

Key Terms
العين (evil eye)
الانقباض (spiritual contraction)
جهنم (Hell)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Sanusi's sadness was due to worldly reasons.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states his sadness is from the vision of Hell, not worldly matters.

The Final Days of Imam Sanusi: A Lesson in Compassion and Community
A student describes Sanusi's withdrawal from people and his effort to attend mosque despite illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of Imam Sanusi's withdrawal and his compassionate return to the community during his final illness.

Core Idea
True leadership is serving others even in one's own suffering.

In this biographical passage, a student describes the final days of Imam Sanusi. We see a shift from spiritual withdrawal to compassionate engagement. This lesson invites us to reflect on the balance between personal spiritual contraction and communal responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the rhythm of the narrative and the key terms.
Pay attention to the emotional arc: withdrawal, community distress, compassionate response.
2
Understand Inqibad
The term انقباض (spiritual contraction) is central. Understand that this is a natural state for deeply spiritual people, not a negative withdrawal.
Imam Sanusi's earlier custom was openness; this change is due to advanced age and spiritual state.
3
See the Turning Point
Identify when Sanusi learns of the people's distress and decides to force himself to go to the mosque. This shows his empathy and concern.
The phrase 'فصار يتكلف' indicates he made an effort despite difficulty.
4
Reflect on the Lesson
Consider how this applies to your own life. How do we balance personal struggles with serving others? What can we learn from Sanusi's example?
This is a practical takeaway from the biography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته

لندع الشيخ محمد بن عمر الملالي تلميذ الإمام السنوسي يرسم لنا هذه اللوحة وهو يحدثنا عن ساعات اللقاء والرحيل عن هذه الفانية إلى الدار الباقية، قال رحمه الله تعالى:

•
( كان رضي الله عنه في أواخر عمره كثير الانقباض عن الخلق، لا يكاد ينبسط أحد كما كانت عادته قبل ذلك، ويشق عليه الخروج إلى المسجد للإقراء والصلاة، ولا يخرج إليه في بعض الأيام إلا حياءً من الناس الذين ينتظرونه في المسجد للصلاة ولمَّا أحسَّ رضي الله تعالى عنه بألم مرضه الذي توفي منه . . انقطع عن المسجد، فسمع الناس بمرضه، فصاروا يأتون إلى المسجد فلا يجدونه، فتتغير قلوبهم من فقدان الشيخ وعدم رؤيته لهم، فأُخبر الشيخ بذلك فصار يتكلف الخروج إلى المسجد للصلاة لأجل الناس، فإذا رأوه فرحوا وسُرُّوا بخروجه ورؤيته

•
فخرج يوماً وأتى لباب المسجد، وأراد الصعود إليه، فلم يقدر)
The last panel of his life: Let us ask Shaykh Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, the student of Imam al-Sanusi, to draw for us this panel as he tells us about the hours of meeting and departure from this transitory world to the abiding abode. He said, may Allah have mercy on him:

•
(He, may Allah be pleased with him, was in the latter part of his life often contracted away from people, hardly anyone would open up as was his custom before that, and going out to the mosque for teaching and prayer was difficult for him, and he would not go out on some days except out of shyness from the people who were waiting for him in the mosque for prayer. And when he, may Allah be pleased with him, felt the pain of his illness from which he died, he cut off from the mosque. The people heard of his illness, and they began to come to the mosque but did not find him, so their hearts became disturbed by the loss of the Shaykh and not seeing him. The Shaykh was informed of that, and so he began to force himself to go out to the mosque for prayer for the sake of the people. When they saw him, they rejoiced and were delighted by his going out and seeing him.

•
Then one day he went out and came to the door of the mosque, and he wanted to ascend to it, but he could not.)
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account by a student (Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali) of Imam Sanusi's final days. It begins by setting the scene: the student will describe the 'last panel' of the Imam's life. The narrative then describes Sanusi's increasing withdrawal from people (inkibad) in his later years, such that he rarely interacted as he used to. Going to the mosque for teaching and prayer became burdensome, and he only attended sometimes out of shyness towards the people waiting. When he fell terminally ill, he stopped going to the mosque altogether. The people, distressed, came to the mosque but did not find him. When Sanusi learned of their sadness, he forced himself to go out to the mosque for their sake, and the people rejoiced at seeing him. The passage ends with a poignant scene: one day he came to the mosque door but could not ascend (presumably due to weakness). This account highlights Sanusi's compassion and his concern for the community even in his final illness.

Key Terms
الانقباض

المسجد
الشفقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Sanusi's withdrawal from people was due to dislike or misanthropy.
Correction: Sanusi's withdrawal was a spiritual state called inqibad (contraction), a natural occurrence for deeply spiritual individuals, and not due to any dislike. His later actions, forcing himself to attend for the people, show his compassion.

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Sanusi force himself to go to the mosque during his final illness?
A) Because he was ordered by the caliph
B) Because he needed to teach an important lesson
C) Because he heard the people were sad and missing him
D) Because it was a religious obligation he always fulfilled

Key Takeaway
In his final illness, Imam Sanusi's compassion for his community moved him to overcome his own physical weakness and spiritual withdrawal to be present for them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when your own discomfort or pain made you want to withdraw from others. Did you consider how your absence might affect them? How does Sanusi's example of forcing himself for their sake speak to your own choices? What would you do differently now?
?
If the people around you were distressed by not seeing you, would that move you to action? Why or why not? What does your answer reveal about your priorities and your love for others?
?
Sanusi's compassion cost him effort and physical strain. Are you willing to sacrifice your comfort for the joy of others, even when you are suffering? What holds you back, and what would help you take that step?

The Final Days of Imam al-Sanusi: Illness and Death
Al-Mallali narrates the final days of Imam al-Sanusi, including his illness, last prayer, and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical account of Imam al-Sanusi's final days, including his physical struggles, spiritual steadfastness, and the lessons from his last prayer and vision.

Core Idea
Even in severe illness, Imam al-Sanusi remained devoted to worship and sound in faith.

This lesson presents a moving narrative from Shaykh Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, a student of Imam al-Sanusi, describing the imam's last days. It shows how he struggled to attend the mosque for prayer despite his weakness, and how he remained conscious and faithful even when visited by angels. The story offers a profound example of perseverance and submission to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about his withdrawal
Start by reading how Imam al-Sanusi became withdrawn and found it difficult to go to the mosque. Notice his concern for the people's feelings.
This sets the stage for his physical decline.
2
Focus on his last prayer
Read about the Asr prayer on Friday that he led with great difficulty. This was his final public prayer.
Observe his determination to pray despite exhaustion.
3
Examine the vision and conversation
After losing consciousness, he tells his wife about being taken by angels and hearing a command to leave 'that prison'. Understand this as a spiritual experience.
The exact meaning of 'that prison' is not specified in the text, but it signifies a worldly attachment.
4
Review the final ten days
Read how he remained bedridden for ten days, his condition worsening, yet he kept a sound mind and did not complain.
This reflects his patience and trust in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللوحة الأخيرة من حياته
لندع الشيخ محمد بن عمر الملالي تلميذ الإمام السنوسي يرسم لنا هذه اللوحة وهو يحدثنا عن ساعات اللقاء والرحيل عن هذه الفانية إلى الدار الباقية ، قال رحمه الله تعالى :
( كان رضي الله عنه في أواخر عمره كثير الانقباض عن الخلق ، لا يكاد ينبسط أحد كما كانت عادته قبل ذلك ، ويشق عليه الخروج إلى المسجد للإقراء والصلاة ، ولا يخرج إليه في بعض الأيام إلا حياءً من الناس الذين ينتظرونه في المسجد للصلاة ، ولمَّا أحسَّ رضي الله تعالى عنه بألم مرضه الذي توفي منه . . انقطع عن المسجد ، فسمع الناس بمرضه ، فصاروا يأتون إلى المسجد فلا يجدونه ، فتتغير قلوبهم من فقدان الشيخ وعدم رؤيته لهم ، فأُخبر الشيخ بذلك فصار يتكلف الخروج إلى المسجد للصلاة لأجل الناس ، فإذا رأوه فرحوا وسُرُّوا بخروجه ورؤيته .
فخرج يوماً وأتى لباب المسجد ، وأراد الصعود إليه ، فلم يقدر (١) فقال : كيف أطلع إلى المسجد يا ربِّ ؛ أو كما قال ، فهم بالرجوع إلى داره فبدا له خوفاً من أن يدخل على الناس حزناً برجوعه ، فتكلف الصعود إلى المسجد ، وصلى بالناس صلاة عصر يوم الجمعة ، ولم يكمل الصلاة إلا بشق النفس ، وهذه آخر صلاة صلاها .
فرجع إلى داره ، فبقي إلى صبيحة يوم السبت من الغد ، فقربت إليه زوجته طعاماً ، فقال لها : لا أقدر على شيء(١) ، فقالت له : وأي شيء بك ؟ فقال لها : أنا تخلفت ، ثم غاب عن حسّه ، فبقي على تلك الحالة النهار كله .
ثم كلمته زوجته وقالت له : ما الذي غيَّبك عن حِسّكَ ؟ أو قريباً من هذا ، فقال لها : إنَّ الملائكة قد صعدت بي إلى السماء الدنيا ، فسمعت قائلاً يقول لي : اترك ما أنت عليه فقد قَرُبَ أجلُكَ ، ثم قال : لا أستطيع أن أفسر لك بقية ما رأيت ، أو كما قال .
فقالت له زوجته : وما الذي أُمرت بتركه ؟ قال لها : قد تركت ذلك المحبس لا آخذ منه شيئاً أبداً .
ثم إنه لازم الفراش من حينئذ إلى أن توفي ، ومدة مرضه عشرة أيام ، وفي كل ساعة يتقوَّى مرضه وتضاعف ألمه وتضعف قوته وحركته ، ويثقل لسانه ، وهو مع ذلك ثابت العقل [لم] يتأوَّه ولا أنَّ .
(١) وقد ذكر الملالي في ( المواهب القدسية » ( ق ١٣ ) أنه بلغ به الجهد حتى عجز عن التيمم وأركان الصلاة ، فقال : ( رحم الله تعالى أبا حنيفة حيث قال بسقوطها ) يعني : الصلاة .
The final picture of his life:
Let us ask Shaykh Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, the student of Imam al-Sanusi, to draw for us this picture as he tells us about the moments of meeting and departure from this transient world to the everlasting abode. He, may Allah have mercy on him, said:
He, may Allah be pleased with him, was in the last part of his life very withdrawn from people; hardly anyone could cheer him up as had been his habit before that. Going out to the mosque for teaching and prayer was difficult for him, and he would not go out on some days except out of shyness of the people who were waiting for him in the mosque for prayer. When he, may Allah be pleased with him, felt the pain of the illness from which he died, he stopped going to the mosque. The people heard of his illness and began coming to the mosque and not finding him, so their hearts changed due to the loss of the shaykh and not seeing him. The shaykh was informed of that, so he began to force himself to go out to the mosque for prayer for the sake of the people. When they saw him, they rejoiced and were delighted by his coming out and seeing him.
Then one day he went out and came to the door of the mosque, wanting to ascend into it, but he could not (1). He said: 'How can I go up to the mosque, O Lord?' or as he said. Then he intended to return to his house, but it occurred to him, fearing that he would cause sadness to the people by his return, so he forced himself to ascend to the mosque and led the people in the 'Asr prayer on Friday. He did not complete the prayer except with great difficulty, and this was the last prayer he prayed.
He returned to his house and remained until the morning of the next day, Saturday. His wife brought food near him, and he said to her: 'I cannot [eat] anything. He said to her: 'I cannot [eat] anything (1).' She said to him: 'What is the matter with you?' He said to her: 'I have fallen short.' Then he lost consciousness, and he remained in that state the whole day.
Then his wife spoke to him and said: 'What has taken away your consciousness?' or close to this. He said to her: 'Indeed, the angels ascended with me to the lowest heaven, and I heard a speaker saying to me: 'Leave what you are upon, for your term has drawn near.' Then he said: 'I cannot explain to you the rest of what I saw,' or as he said.
His wife said to him: 'And what were you commanded to leave?' He said to her: 'I have left that prison; I will never take anything from it again.'
Then he kept to his bed from then until he died. The duration of his illness was ten days. Every hour his illness grew stronger, his pain multiplied, his strength and movement weakened, and his tongue became heavy. Yet despite that, he was of sound mind; he did not groan nor did he ...[possibly 'complain'?].
(1) Al-Mallali mentioned in (al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya, folio 13) that the effort reached him to the point that he was unable to perform tayammum and the pillars of prayer, so he said: 'May Allah have mercy on Abu Hanifa, who declared them to be dropped,' meaning the prayer.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative by Muhammad ibn Umar al-Mallali, a student of Imam al-Sanusi, describing the final days of the imam's life. It serves as a historical account of his illness, his last public prayer, and his death, highlighting his spiritual state and physical suffering. The narrative begins by describing al-Sanusi's withdrawal from people in his old age, contrasting with his earlier sociability. It emphasizes that despite his weakness, he forced himself to go to the mosque for prayer out of consideration for the people who missed him. The account of his last prayer—the 'Asr prayer on Friday—shows his determination; he could barely climb the steps and completed the prayer only with great difficulty. After returning home, he lost consciousness, and his wife conversed with him about his vision. He reported being taken by angels to the lowest heaven, where a voice commanded him to leave something he was attached to, which he later identified as 'that prison' (المحبس). The narrative then states that he remained bedridden for ten days, his condition worsening daily, yet he remained conscious and did not groan or complain. The footnote adds that his weakness became so severe that he could not perform even the essential acts of prayer, leading him to invoke the ruling of Abu Hanifa that exemption from prayer is permissible in such states. This biography illustrates the physical frailty and spiritual steadfastness of Imam al-Sanusi at the end of his life.

Correction: The narrative actually illustrates the imam's adherence to the law and his spiritual state even in illness, showing the importance of perseverance in worship and trust in Allah.

C) 'I have left the world; I will never return to it.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine yourself in Imam al-Sanusi's state: severe pain, unable to even perform the pillars of prayer. Would you trust Allah's mercy enough to accept the dispensation without guilt, or would you feel you must force yourself beyond your capacity? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of Allah's mercy in the shari'ah?
?
The imam said 'I have left that prison; I will never take anything from it again.' What is the 'prison' in your own life that you know you must leave but are clinging to? What would it look like to truly abandon it today, not tomorrow?
?
When the imam heard the command to leave what he was upon, he did not hesitate. If you were given a direct command from Allah through a vision or clear sign, would you obey immediately? What does your hesitation in obeying known commands (like prayer, charity, or leaving sin) say about your readiness for that moment?
