The Death of a Righteous Servant
Narrates the final days and death of a devout scholar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a true believer's death combines intense physical suffering with unwavering spiritual awareness and trust in Allah.

Core Idea
Righteous believers endure death's pain with patience and faith, not without suffering.

This passage offers a vivid, intimate account of the final days of a devout scholar (likely a Wali). It shows that the death of a righteous person is not free from pain, but rather marked by profound patience, clarity of mind, and a heart fully turned to Allah. As you read, notice the balance between physical ordeal and spiritual steadfastness.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative closely
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, following the sequence from the last prayer to the final moments. Notice the vivid details.
This is a biographical account, not a doctrinal argument—let the story speak.
2
Identify key stages of death
The narrative describes several stages: the final prayer, the vision, the ten-day illness, the death pangs, the prompting of shahada, and the final words. List these stages.
Each stage reveals something about the believer's state.
3
Contrast physical suffering and spiritual stability
Observe how his body weakens and he experiences severe pain, yet his mind remains clear and he is aware of Allah. Why is this significant?
It refutes the misconception that righteous people die easily.
4
Reflect on the theological message
Consider what this narrative teaches about death, dying, and the afterlife. How does the sheikh's response to his daughter and the shahada prompting show his faith?
This is a practical lesson in tazkiyah (spiritual purification).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : كيف أطلع إلى المسجد يا ربِّ ؛ أو كما قال ، فهم بالرجوع إلى داره فبدا له خوفاً من أن يدخل على الناس حزناً برجوعه ، فتكلف الصعود إلى المسجد ، وصلى بالناس صلاة عصر يوم الجمعة ، ولم يكمل الصلاة إلا بشق النفس ، وهذه آخر صلاة صلاها فرجع إلى داره ، فبقي إلى صبيحة يوم السبت من الغد ، فقربت إليه زوجته طعاماً ، فقال لها : لا أقدر على شيء(۱) ، فقالت له : وأي شيء بك ؟ فقال لها : أنا تخلفت ، ثم غاب عن حسّه ، فبقي على تلك الحالة النهار كله ثم كلمته زوجته وقالت له : ما الذي غيَّبك عن حِسّكَ ؟ أو قريباً من ذلك ، فقال لها : إنَّ الملائكة قد صعدت بي إلى السماء الدنيا ، فسمعت قائلاً يقول لي : اترك ما أنت عليه فقد قَرُبَ أجلُكَ ، ثم قال : لا أستطيع أن أفسر لك بقية ما رأيت ، أو كما قال فقالت له زوجته : وما الذي أُمرت بتركه ؟ قال لها : قد تركت ذلك المحبس لا آخذ منه شيئاً أبداً . ثم إنه لازم الفراش من حينئذ إلى أن توفي ، ومدة مرضه عشرة أيام ، وفي كل ساعة يتقوَّى مرضه وتضاعف ألمه وتضعف قوته وحركته ، ويثقل لسانه ، وهو مع ذلك ثابت العقل [لم] يتأوَّه ولا أنَّ (۱) وقد ذكر الملالي في ( المواهب القدسية » ( ق ۱۳ ) أنه بلغ به الجهد حتى عجز عن التيمم وأركان الصلاة ، فقال : ( رحم الله تعالى أبا حنيفة حيث قال بسقوطها ) يعني : الصلاة بالكلية ، ثم تجده مع ذلك يكلّم من كلَّمه ، ويسلم على من سلَّم عليه فلمَّا قَرُب أجلُهُ بثلاثة أيام دخلته سكرات الموت ، فرجع يتأوه بالقهر ويميل يميناً وشمالاً ، فنظرت إليه وقد احمرت وجنتاه وأرخيت عيناه وشفتاه (۱) ، واشتدَّ نَفَسُهُ ، وتقوى صعوده وهبوطه ، فلم أملك صبراً على البكاء بما عاينت من شدة مقاساته وعظيم صبره على ذلك ، ففارقته وظننتُ أنه لا يبقى تلك الليلة ، وكانت ليلة السبت ، فبقي في النزع تلك الليلة والأحد إلى بعد العصر ، فكان ابن أخيه حينئذ يلقنه الشهادة مرة بعد مرة ، فالتفت الشيخ له وقال بكلام ضعيف جداً : وهل ثَمَّ غيرها ؟! يعني : أنه رضي الله تعالى عنه ليس بغافل عنها بقلبه في هذا الوقت ، وإن كنت لم أنطق بها اللسان فحينئذٍ استبشروا بذلك ، وعرف الحاضرون أنه ثابت العقل ، ليس بغافل عن الله سبحانه وكانت بنته رضي الله عنها تقول له حينئذٍ : تمشي وتتركني ؟! فقال لها : الجنة تجمعنا عن قريب إن شاء الله تعالى وكانت في يده رضي الله تعالى عنه سبحة ، فلما اشتدَّ مرضه سقطت السبحة من يده ، فبقي كذلك ما شاء الله ، ثم التفت إلى السبحة فلم يجدها في يده ، فقال : ذهبت العبادة يا محمد ؛ يعني : نفسه وكان رضي الله عنه يقول عند موته : نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا (۱) وانظر هنا « المواهب القدسية » ( ق ٩٣ )
He said: 'How can I go up to the mosque, O Lord?' or as he said. Then he intended to return to his home, but it occurred to him that he would cause sorrow to the people by his return, so he forced himself to climb to the mosque and led the people in the afternoon prayer on Friday, and he did not complete the prayer except with great difficulty, and this was the last prayer he prayed. Then he returned to his home and remained until the morning of the following Saturday. His wife brought him food, and he said to her: 'I cannot eat anything.' She said to him: 'What is the matter with you?' He said to her: 'I have fallen behind.' Then he lost consciousness, and remained in that state the whole day. Then his wife spoke to him and said: 'What has taken away your senses?' or something close to that. He said to her: 'The angels ascended with me to the lowest heaven, and I heard a speaker say to me: Leave what you are doing, for your time has drawn near.' Then he said: 'I cannot explain to you the rest of what I saw,' or as he said. His wife said to him: 'What were you commanded to leave?' He said to her: 'I have left that prison; I will never take anything from it again.' Then from that time he took to his bed until he died, and the duration of his illness was ten days. Every hour his illness worsened, his pain doubled, his strength and movement weakened, and his tongue grew heavy. Yet he remained of sound mind, not groaning or moaning. (1) Al-Malali mentioned in 'Al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' (folio 13) that he was so exhausted that he was unable to perform tayammum or the pillars of prayer, and he said: 'May Allah have mercy on Abu Hanifa for holding that they are dropped' meaning the prayer entirely. Then despite that, you would find him speaking to whoever spoke to him and returning greetings to whoever greeted him. When his time drew near by three days, the pangs of death came upon him, and he began to groan with distress and lean right and left. I looked at him and saw his cheeks had reddened, his eyes had drooped, and his lips had slackened, his breathing became intense, and his rising and falling became strong. I could not control my tears from crying because of what I witnessed of the severity of his ordeal and the greatness of his patience in it. So I left him, thinking he would not survive that night, which was a Saturday. He remained in agony that night and on Sunday until after the afternoon. Then his nephew began to prompt him with the testimony of faith (shahada) time after time. The sheikh turned to him and said in a very weak voice: 'Is there anything else besides it?' meaning that he, may Allah be pleased with him, was not careless of it in his heart at that time, even if he had not pronounced it with his tongue. At that they rejoiced, and those present knew that he was of sound mind and not heedless of Allah, Glorified and Exalted. His daughter, may Allah be pleased with her, said to him then: 'You are leaving and abandoning me?!' He said to her: 'Paradise will gather us soon, Allah willing.' He had a prayer beads in his hand, but when his illness intensified the beads fell from his hand. He remained like that for as long as Allah willed, then he looked for the beads but did not find them in his hand, and said: 'Worship has gone, O Muhammad' meaning himself. He, may Allah be pleased with him, used to say at his death: 'We ask Him, Glorified and Exalted, to make us...' (1) See here 'Al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya' (folio 93).
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a biographical narrative describing the final days and death of a righteous person, likely a scholar. It begins with his struggle to reach the mosque for the last Friday prayer, showing his commitment despite extreme weakness. After returning home, he loses consciousness and later reveals a vision: angels took him to the lowest heaven where he was told his time is near and to leave something he was attached to (likely worldly concerns or a particular practice). His wife's questions elicit his obedience. The narrative then describes his ten-day illness, noting his patience and clear-mindedness even as his body weakens. Three days before death, the death pangs begin, causing groaning and physical changes. The observer struggles to control tears due to the severity but notes the sufferer's patience. The nephew's prompting of the shahada is met with a reply indicating the sheikh is already mindful of it in his heart, demonstrating that true remembrance is not merely verbal. His daughter's lament is answered with hope in Paradise. Finally, when his prayer beads fall, he remarks that worship has departed, a poignant expression of his focus on devotion. The passage concludes with his final words, asking Allah to make him among the righteous. Throughout, the account emphasizes the state of a believer's end: suffering, patience, prophetic traditions (like the death pangs of the Prophet), and steadfast faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The dying scholar was more troubled by the falling of his prayer beads than by his own agony. What does your attachment to worship reveal about the state of your soul? If you were in his place, what would you grieve losing?
?
This narrative shows that even the most righteous experience intense pain at death. Are you living as if you will be exempt from hardship? How does this truth reshape your understanding of Allah's mercy and your need for patience? What will you change in your daily life to build that patience now?

The Sheikh's Death Throes and Steadfast Shahada
Account of death throes and shahada exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sheikh's death throes displayed his unwavering faith and awareness of Allah.

Core Idea
Even in death's agony, the sheikh's heart was fully conscious of the Shahada.

This passage takes us to the sheikh's bedside during his final three days. The narrator describes the physical signs of death and the sheikh's remarkable patience. It is a story of spiritual steadfastness that instructs us on the value of being mindful of Allah at the moment of death.

Guided Path
1
Read the death throes description
Read the Arabic passage describing the onset of death pangs and the narrator's observation. Note the physical changes and the narrator's emotional response.
Focus on the sheikh's patience despite severe suffering.
2
Identify the shahada prompting
The nephew repeatedly prompts the sheikh with the shahada. Notice the sheikh's weak but profound reply: 'Is there anything else besides it?'
The sheikh indicates he is already mindful of the testimony.
3
Reflect on internal vs. external awareness
Consider how the sheikh's response shows that true remembrance can be inward even if the tongue is weak. The narrator and others are reassured of his sound mind.
This counters the misconception that weak speech means lack of consciousness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلمَّا قَرُب أجلُهُ بثلاثة أيام دخلته سكرات الموت ، فرجع يتأوه بالقهر ويميل يميناً وشمالاً ، فنظرت إليه وقد احمرت وجنتاه وأرخيت عيناه وشفتاه (١) ، واشتدَّ نَفَسُهُ ، وتقوى صعوده وهبوطه ، فلم صبراً على البكاء بما عاينت من شدة مقاساته وعظيم صبره على ذلك ، ففارقته وظننتُ أنه لا يبقى تلك الليلة ، وكانت ليلة السبت ، فبقي في النزع تلك الليلة والأحد إلى بعد العصر ، فكان ابن أخيه حينئذ يلقنه الشهادة مرة بعد مرة ، فالتفت الشيخ له وقال بكلام ضعيف جداً : وهل ثَمَّ غيرها ؟! يعني : أنه رضي الله تعالى عنه ليس بغافل عنها بقلبه في هذا الوقت ، وإن كنت لم أنطق بها اللسان. فحينئذٍ استبشروا بذلك ، وعرف الحاضرون أنه ثابت العقل ، ليس بغافل عن الله سبحانه. وكانت بنته رضي الله عنها تقول له حينئذٍ : تمشي وتتركني ؟! فقال لها : الجنة تجمعنا عن قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. وكانت في يده رضي الله تعالى عنه سبحة ، فلما اشتدَّ مرضه سقطت السبحة من يده ، فبقي كذلك ما شاء الله ، ثم التفت إلى السبحة فلم يجدها في يده ، فقال : مشت العبادة يا محمد ؛ يعني : نفسه. وكان رضي الله عنه يقول عند موته : نسأله سبحانه أن يجعلنا وأحبتنا عند الموت ناطقين بكلمتي الشهادة عالمين بها عنه. توفي رحمه الله تعالى ورضي يوم الأحد بعد العصر ، الثامن عشر من جمادى الآخرة ، من عام خمسة وتسعين بعد ثمان مئة. وأخبرتني والدتي رحمها الله تعالى عن بنت الشيخ رضي الله عنها أنها شمت رائحة المسك في البيت بنَفَس موت أبيها ، وشمَّته أيضاً جسده ، والله تعالى أعلم. نسأله سبحانه أن يُقدس روحه ، وأن يسكنه في أعالي الفردوس فسيحه ، وأن يجعله ممَّن يتنعم في كل لحظة برؤية ذات العلية العديمة النظير والمثال ، وأن ينفعنا به في الدنيا والآخرة ، وأن يجمعنا معه بفضله وكرمه في أعلى المنازل الفاخرة ، بجاه سيدنا ونبينا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وعلى آله عدد خلقه ، ورضا نفسه ، وزنة عرشه ، ومداد كلماته )(١). قال العلامة أبو جعفر البلوي : ( ودفن بين ظهري يوم الاثنين بعده حذاء قبر أخيه الصالح العلامة أبي الحسن التانلوتي قدس الله تعالى روحه بعين وانزوته خارج باب الجياد ، حضرنا جنازته ، وكانت في غاية الحفول ؛ غصَّت الشوارع فيها بالناس ، وحضرها السلطان فمن دونه ، وأتبع ثناءً يليق مثله ، وتأسف الناس لفقده وبحق ، وكانت )
When his appointed time approached by three days, the throes of death came upon him. He began to moan with distress and sway right and left. I looked at him and his cheeks had reddened, his eyes and lips had slackened, and his breathing became severe, his rising and falling intensified. I had no patience for weeping because of what I witnessed of the intensity of his struggles and his great patience during that. So I left him and thought that he would not survive that night. It was Saturday night. He remained in the agony of death that night and on Sunday until after the afternoon prayer. His nephew was then prompting him with the shahada repeatedly. The sheikh turned to him and said with very weak speech: 'Is there anything else besides it?' Meaning: that he, may God be pleased with him, was not heedless of it in his heart at that time, even if I did not utter it with the tongue. At that moment they were delighted by that, and those present knew that he was of sound mind, not heedless of God Almighty. His daughter, may God be pleased with her, was saying to him then: 'You will walk and leave me?' So he said to her: 'Paradise will gather us soon, if God Almighty wills.' He had a prayer bead in his hand, may God Almighty be pleased with him. When his illness intensified, the prayer bead fell from his hand. He remained like that for as long as God willed. Then he looked for the prayer bead but did not find it in his hand, so he said: 'Worship has walked, O Muhammad'; meaning: himself. He, may God be pleased with him, used to say at the time of his death: 'We ask Him Almighty to make us and our loved ones at death utterers of the two words of testimony, knowing them.' He passed away, may God have mercy on him and be pleased with him, on Sunday after the afternoon prayer, the 18th of Jumada al-Akhirah, of the year 895. My mother, may God have mercy on her, told me from the sheikh's daughter, may God be pleased with her, that she smelled the scent of musk in the house at the passing of her father's soul, and his body also smelled of it, and God knows best. We ask Him Almighty to sanctify his soul, to lodge him in the highest expanses of Paradise, to make him among those who delight at every moment in the vision of His exalted, peerless, and matchless essence, to benefit us through him in this world and the next, and to gather us with him by His bounty and generosity in the highest luxurious abodes, for the sake of our master, our prophet, and our patron Muhammad, may God bless him and his family, as many as the number of His creation, and His own pleasure, and the weight of His throne, and the ink of His words. The scholar Abu Ja'far al-Balawi said: 'He was buried between noon on Monday following, next to the grave of his righteous brother, the scholar Abu al-Hasan al-Tanluti, may God Almighty sanctify his soul, in 'Ayn Wanzo outside Bab al-Jiyad. We attended his funeral, and it was extremely crowded; the streets were thronged with people, and the sultan and those below him attended, and he was eulogized with befitting praise, and the people mourned his loss, and rightly so. It was...'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a biography, specifically a detailed account of the sheikh's final days, death, and burial. The narrator begins by describing the onset of the pangs of death three days before his passing. The sheikh moaned and swayed, and the narrator observed physical changes like reddened cheeks and slackened eyes and lips. Despite the severity, the narrator notes the sheikh's patience. The narrator left, expecting death that Saturday night, but the sheikh lingered. On Sunday, his nephew prompted him with the shahada, but the sheikh, though weak in speech, indicated he was already mindful of it. This demonstrated his awareness and devotion until the end. His daughter asked why he would leave her, and he consoled her that they would reunite in Paradise. When his prayer bead fell, he remarked that worship had 'walked' from him, showing his constant spiritual engagement. He then uttered a prayer that he and his loved ones might die uttering the shahada. The date of death is given: Sunday, 18 Jumada al-Akhirah 895 AH. A miraculous sign is reported: the scent of musk filled the house and the sheikh's body at death. The passage concludes with a supplication for his soul and details of his burial, which was attended by many including the sultan, and he was buried next to his brother.

سكرات الموت
شهادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sheikh's weak speech might be mistaken for lack of consciousness or heedlessness.
Correction: His reply shows he was fully aware and mindful of the shahada internally, even if unable to articulate it clearly.

Checkpoint
What did the sheikh say when his nephew prompted him with the shahada?
A) "I bear witness"
B) "Is there anything else besides it?"
C) "Leave me"
D) "Paradise awaits"

Key Takeaway
True remembrance of Allah can be inward even when outward expression is weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are alone and facing your own final moments, will your heart be as absorbed in the shahada as the sheikh's was? What are you doing today to make that your natural state at death?
?
The narrator left because he could not bear the intensity of the death throes. Are you avoiding the reality of death because it is uncomfortable? What would change if you confronted it daily?
?
If someone prompted you with the shahada at your deathbed, would your response reveal a heart already immersed in it, or would it expose a neglect you've been ignoring?

Final conversations, prayer bead, and burial details

Learning Objective

Core Idea
The sheikh's life was so immersed in worship that even falling beads prompted a profound reflection.

The sheikh's final moments were filled with intimate conversations: his daughter's loving plea, his own supplication, and a small incident with a prayer bead that reveals his constant awareness of worship. The passage concludes with accounts of his burial, a miraculous fragrance, and a prayer for his soul.

Guided Path

He sees his inability to hold the bead as a loss of worship opportunity.
3

He prays that he and his loved ones may die uttering the shahada with knowledge. This shows his concern for the end of life.
4

The passage records the date of death, the scent of musk, and the grand funeral. Consider how these elements affirm his sanctity.

Eulogy for Imam Sanusi by al-Hawdi
A eulogy poem praising Imam Sanusi's virtues and legacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the poetic eulogy of Imam Sanusi expresses reverence for his spiritual and scholarly legacy.

Core Idea
A eulogy reveals the depth of reverence scholars held for Imam Sanusi.

This passage is a heartfelt poetic eulogy composed by the jurist al-Hawdi after Imam Sanusi's death. It uses vivid imagery to highlight the imam's role as an inheritor of prophetic knowledge and a source of healing for spiritual ailments. As you read, notice the poet's emotional tone and the attributes he ascribes to the imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic poem and translation
First, read the Arabic eulogy silently, then compare it with the literal translation. Notice the rhythm and poetic imagery.
The poem is in the complete meter (كامل).
2
Identify the key praises
List the titles and descriptions the poet uses for Imam Sanusi, such as 'heir of the Prophet', 'unique of scholars', and 'pearl of ascetics'. Consider why these are significant.
3
Reflect on the purpose of eulogies
Think about why scholars composed eulogies for each other in Islamic history. How does this poem serve to honor the imam and inspire future generations?
This connects to the broader tradition of literary tributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما رثاه الفقيه الأجل أبو عبد الله محمد بن عبد الرحمن الحوضي بعد وفاته (۲) :
( من الكامل )
ما للمنازل أظلمَتْ أرجاؤُها والأرضِ رجتْ حينَ خَابَ رجاؤُها هذا الذي ورثَ النبيَّ فأصبحَتْ علل الضلال به استفيد دواؤها هذا الذي تبعَ النبي وصحبَهُ فانجابَ عن سُبل الهدى ظلماؤُها يا أيُّها النفس المقدسة التي لبقائها المحمودِ كَانَ فَناؤُها يا أوحد العلماء يا عَلَماً بهِ كَلُّ العلوم بدَتْ لنا أنحاؤُها يا درَّةَ الزهّادِ یا غوثاً بهِ يُرجى لأمراض القلوب شفاؤها أخلاقُكَ التسليم يصحبها الرضا بالله منشور عليكَ لـواؤُها بلت ترابه سحب الرضوان ، وجعل الله مستقره فراديس الجنان
And among what the distinguished jurist Abu Abdullah Muhammad bin Abd al-Rahman al-Hawdi composed as a eulogy after his death (2):
(From the complete meter):
What is with the dwellings? Their corners have darkened, and the earth trembled when its hope was disappointed. This is the one who inherited the Prophet, so the ailments of misguidance have found their cure through him. This is the one who followed the Prophet and his Companions, so the darknesses of guidance's paths cleared away. O sanctified soul, whose annihilation was for its praiseworthy endurance. O unique of the scholars, O banner by which all sciences have revealed their directions to us. O pearl of the ascetics, O succor by whom healing is hoped for heart diseases. Your ethics are accompanied by submission and contentment with God; your banner is unfurled over you. The clouds of [God's] pleasure have watered his dust, and God has made his resting place in the gardens of Paradise.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a poetic eulogy (رثاء) composed by the jurist Muhammad bin Abd al-Rahman al-Hawdi after the death of Imam al-Sanusi. The poem is in the complete meter (كامل). The poet begins by describing a darkened world and trembling earth upon the loss of the Imam, then identifies the Imam as the one who inherited the Prophet (ورث النبي), meaning he carried on the prophetic legacy, so that the ailments of misguidance found their cure through him. He then praises the Imam's following of the Prophet and Companions, dispelling darkness from the paths of guidance. The poet addresses the Imam's soul as sanctified, and calls him the unique of scholars (أوحد العلماء) and a banner (علم) through which all sciences have revealed their directions. He calls him the pearl of ascetics (درة الزهاد) and a succor (غوث) from whom healing is hoped for heart diseases. The Imam's ethics are described as accompanied by submission (تسليم) and contentment with God (رضا بالله), and the poet prays that God's pleasure waters his dust and grants him Paradise. The poem highlights the Imam's spiritual and intellectual status.

Key Terms
رثاء
أوحد العلماء

The Five Creedal Works of Imam al-Sanusi
Historians praise and list al-Sanusi's five creedal works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the names and praise of Imam al-Sanusi's five creedal works as recorded by historians.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's five creeds are praised as unmatched masterpieces of Islamic creedal writing.

The passage opens with a glowing evaluation from two historians. First, al-Shafshawni declares that al-Sanusi's five creeds and their commentaries are among the best works ever authored in Islam. He then lists them by name. Second, Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi states that no later scholar produced anything comparable. This lesson introduces you to these five foundational texts that form the Sanusiyyat series.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical praise
Read the Arabic passage containing al-Shafshawni's statement. Notice the confidence in calling these works among the best in Islam.
2
Identify the five names
Locate the five names: al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, al-Kubra. Understand the order from shortest to longest.
3
Consider Ibn al-Qadi's comment
Read the second historian's comment that no later scholar produced anything like these creeds. Reflect on the high praise.
This sets the stage for their importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في السلسلة العقدية للامام السنوسي قال العلامة المؤرخ الشفشاوني في الإمام السنوسي : ( وعقائده الخمس وشروحاتها مِنْ أفضل ما أُلف في الإسلام ؛ وهي المقدمة » ، و « الصغرى » ، و « صغرى الصغرى » ، و« الوسطى » ، و الكبرى ( ( (۱) ، وهي كلمة ذهبية في تقويم ( السنوسيات ) واكتفى المؤرخ ابن القاضي المكناسي في التعريف بالإمام السنوسي بقوله : ( صاحبُ العقائد التي لم يأتِ أحد بمثلها من المتأخرين ((٢) وسلسلة « السنوسيات ( بشروحها ممَّا تشبث بذاكرة التاريخ فما كانت لتنسى ، ورغم زحمة التأليف العقدية وتنوعها حجماً ومنهجاً ، ودخولها مكتبة علم الكلام بكل ثقة ورصانة . . كان لـ « السنوسيات » أعظم الأثر في الغرب الإسلامي ثم شرقه ؛ إذ دخلت بقدم راسخة سلسلة التعليم المنهجي لعلم الكلام ؛ بما تميزت عن غيرها من كتب الاعتقاد بالابتداء بمعتصر المختصر الذي تجده في وجيز متونها ، إلى المختصر الذي تراه في كبير المتون ووجيز الشروح ، إلى الوسيط ثم إلى سعة البسيط (۱) انظر ( دوحة الناشر » ( ص ١٢١ ) (۲) انظر » درة الحجال » ( ٢/ ١٤١ )
In the creedal series of Imam al-Sanusi. The historian al-Shafshawni said concerning Imam al-Sanusi: 'His five creeds and their commentaries are among the best works authored in Islam; they are: al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra.' This is a golden statement in evaluating the Sanusiyyat. The historian Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi sufficed in introducing Imam al-Sanusi by saying: 'The author of the creeds for which none among the later scholars has produced anything like.' The series of the Sanusiyyat with its commentaries is among those things that have clung to the memory of history and could not be forgotten. Despite the crowding of creedal works and their diversity in size and methodology, and their entry into the library of kalam with full confidence and gravity, the Sanusiyyat had the greatest impact in the Islamic West and then its East; for they entered with a firmly established foot into the systematic educational series of kalam, distinguished from other books of creed by beginning with the most compressed of the abridged, which you find in the concise texts, then to the abridged which you see in the longer texts and concise commentaries, then to the intermediate, then to the extent of the expansive. (1) See 'Dawhat al-Nashir', p. 121. (2) See 'Durrat al-Hijal', 2/141.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an introduction praising Imam al-Sanusi's creedal works. It begins by quoting two historians: al-Shafshawni states that the five creeds and their commentaries are among the best Islamic works, listing them as al-Muqaddimah, al-Sughra, Sughra al-Sughra, al-Wusta, and al-Kubra. Ibn al-Qadi al-Miknasi says that no later scholar produced anything like these creeds. The passage then describes the series' lasting impact, noting that despite the abundance of other creedal works, the Sanusiyyat entered the educational system of kalam with a firm footing. Their unique feature is the graded progression from the most compressed abridgment (found in the concise texts) to the abridged (found in longer texts and concise commentaries), then to the intermediate, and finally to the expansive. This grading is the core pedagogical innovation. The footnotes cite the sources for the two quotations.

?
The historians praise these works as unmatched. Are you truly seeking the most sound and thorough understanding of Islamic creed, or are you satisfied with what is popular? What will you change in your study routine to ensure you are learning from the best sources?

The Graded Pedagogy of the Sanusiyyat
Author describes the graded pedagogy and impact of Sanusiyyat series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique graded pedagogical structure of the Sanusiyyat series and how it contributed to its lasting impact.

Core Idea
The Sanusiyyat series progresses from the most compressed to the most expansive, making it a systematic educational tool.

The passage continues by describing the lasting impact of the Sanusiyyat series. Despite many other creedal works, these texts entered the educational curriculum firmly because of their unique graded progression. The author explains how the series moves from the most compressed abridgment, through intermediate levels, to the fullest exposition. This lesson explores that pedagogical method.

Guided Path
1
Read about the series' impact
Read the Arabic passage that starts with 'وسلسلة السنوسيات'. Note that it clung to memory and could not be forgotten.
2
Identify the graded levels
Look for the description of progression: from the most compressed (معتصر المختصر) to the abridged (المختصر) to the intermediate (الوسيط) to the expansive (سعة البسيط).
3
Reflect on why this method succeeded
Think about how a graded approach suits learners at different levels, making the works adaptable for teaching.
This method is a key feature distinguishing the Sanusiyyat from other creedal books.

Sanusi's Predecessors and His Unique Series
Describes Sanusi's place among earlier kalam scholars and his innovative series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi's work on kalam was both rooted in the tradition of earlier scholars and uniquely innovative in its impact.

Core Idea
Al-Sanusi's series revived kalam by building on past masters while capturing future attention.

This lesson places al-Sanusi's theological series in its proper historical context. It shows that he was not an isolated figure but built directly on the work of earlier giants like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani. Yet what made his series truly remarkable was how it was embraced by scholars even during his lifetime, signaling a new direction for the study of creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from predecessors to the unique aspect.
The passage begins by acknowledging prior scholars, then pivots to what made Sanusi distinct.
2
Identify the two predecessors
Find the names of the two scholars mentioned and the titles of their works. Understand that al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani are from the eastern Islamic world.
This shows Sanusi was part of an ongoing tradition.
3
Spot the novel aspect
Look for the phrase 'but the novel aspect' (ولكن البديع). This signals the key idea: Sanusi's series was not just a repetition but a fresh revival.
The passage claims his series would dominate future teaching.
4
Reflect on the impact
Consider the last sentence: his works were embraced by scholars even during his lifetime. This is presented as evidence of their significance.
This sets Sanusi apart from many scholars whose works gained fame later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد سبق إليه من قِبَلِ أعلام علم الكلام ، فلحجة الإسلام الغزالي مثله ؛ في « قواعد العقائد » و « الرسالة القدسية » و « الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد » ، وللعلامة المحقق سعد الدين التفتازاني شِبْهُهُ ؛ في تهذيب المنطق والكلام » و « المقاصد » و « شرح المقاصد » ، وكلاهما من أقصى المشرق الإسلامي ، ولكن البديع في سلسلة الإمام السنوسي هو استحياؤه وكشفه الإلهامي عن تربع هذه السلسلة المباركة مناحي النظر والتدريس في علم العقائد في المستقبل القريب ، بل إنَّ تأليفه هذه عانقتها أنظار العلماء وأقلامهم في حياته قبل موته
Indeed, he was preceded by the imams of the science of kalam, such as the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali in his 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id', 'al-Risala al-Qudsiyya', and 'al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad'; and the verifying scholar Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani similarly in 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam', 'al-Maqasid', and 'Sharh al-Maqasid', both from the furthest Islamic East. But the novel aspect in the series of Imam al-Sanusi is its revival and inspired unveiling that this blessed series would occupy the fields of investigation and teaching in the science of beliefs in the near future. Rather, his composition [of these works] was embraced by the eyes and pens of scholars during his lifetime before his death.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces the historical context of Sanusi's work on kalam. It begins by acknowledging that he was preceded by major figures: al-Ghazali, who wrote foundational texts like 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id', and al-Taftazani, who authored works such as 'Tahdhib al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam' and 'Sharh al-Maqasid'. Both are from the eastern Islamic world. However, the unique quality of Sanusi's series is highlighted: it seemed to revive and inspire a new future for the teaching of creed ('ilm al-'aqa'id). The passage emphasizes that Sanusi's works were eagerly studied and engaged by scholars even while he was still alive, which was a remarkable achievement.

Key Terms

قواعد العقائد
الاقتصاد في الاعتقاد
تهذيب المنطق والكلام
المقاصد
السنوسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's work on kalam was a completely new invention without any precedent in earlier scholarship.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that he was preceded by great scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani. Sanusi built on their foundations.

Checkpoint
Which two scholars does the passage mention as predecessors of al-Sanusi in writing on kalam?
A) Al-Ash'ari and al-Maturidi
B) Al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani
C) Ibn Rushd and Ibn Sina
D) Al-Baqillani and al-Juwayni

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's series on kalam was rooted in the works of al-Ghazali and al-Taftazani, but its immediate acceptance and predicted dominance in teaching marked it as a unique revival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about 'aqeedah, do you see yourself as part of a living tradition that builds on past scholars, or do you treat it as isolated facts? How can you connect to the legacy of scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Sanusi in your own study?
?
Al-Sanusi's works were so powerful that scholars engaged with them while he was still alive. What kind of legacy are you building with your knowledge? Are you sharing it in a way that could benefit others even in your lifetime?
?
If you were to write a 'series' on a topic you care about, what would you want its unique contribution to be? Are you willing to put in the work to make it a revival rather than just repetition?

Sanusi's Fame and Scholarly Recognition
Reports high esteem scholars held for Sanusi's kalam mastery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the high esteem in which scholars held Imam al-Sanusi's mastery of kalam during his lifetime, as evidenced by a contemporary scholar's praise and the immediate study of his works.

Core Idea
Sanusi's kalam mastery was so acclaimed that it became proverbial even in his own lifetime.

This passage from Sanusi's biography highlights how his expertise in kalam was recognized by his peers and teachers. We see a striking testimonial from Shaykh Abu 'Imran, and the tangible impact of his writings on students and scholars alike. This gives us a vivid picture of the scholarly reception of his work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the content of the praise and the biographical details sink in.
Notice the phrase 'مضرب مثل' (proverbial) — this indicates his status was widely known.
2
Identify the key testimonial
Locate the quote from Shaykh Abu 'Imran and understand what he said about Sanusi. He said he had not seen anyone 'sifted' this science like Sanusi.
The word 'غربل' (sifted) is a metaphor for thorough analysis and clarification.
3
Understand the metaphor of sifting
Reflect on why 'sifting' (gharbala) is a powerful description. It implies separating the essential from the non-essential, clarifying complexities.
4
Note the evidence of acceptance
Observe that before his death, Sanusi saw his works studied by students, and even his teachers engaged with them, extracting a book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'.
This shows his works were not only popular but also respected by senior scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومكانة الإمام في علم الكلام صارت مضرب مثل ، حتى قال الشيخ أبو عمران موسى بن عقدة الأغضاوي إذا ذكر علم الكلام : ( ما رأيتُ مَنْ غربلَ هذا العلم مثل هذا الرجل ) يعني : السنوسي (1) وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية . . قرَّت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها « سنوسياته » ـ قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم ، بل إنَّ بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب حقائق السنوسي
And the status of the Imam in the science of kalam became a proverbial example, such that the Shaykh Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Uqda al-Aghdawi said when the science of kalam was mentioned: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man,' meaning al-Sanusi. And before the departure of our Imam al-Sanusi from this fleeting world, his eye was comforted when he saw his works—especially his 'Sanusiyyat'—approached by students of knowledge. Rather, some of his teachers examined them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the high regard for Sanusi's expertise in kalam. It recounts a saying by Shaykh Abu 'Imran Musa ibn 'Uqda al-Aghdawi that he had never seen anyone who 'sifted' (gharbala) this science as Sanusi did—meaning he thoroughly analyzed and clarified it. His status became proverbial. Furthermore, the passage notes that before his death, he had the joy of seeing his works, especially the 'Sanusiyyat', being studied by students. Even some of his teachers engaged with them, extracting a work called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. This shows the immediate scholarly acceptance and impact of his writings.

Key Terms
مضرب مثل
غربل هذا العلم
سنوسياته
حقائق السنوسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Sanusi's works only gained recognition after his death.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that before his death, his works were already being studied by students and even his teachers extracted a book from them.

Checkpoint
What did Shaykh Abu 'Imran say about al-Sanusi regarding kalam?
A) He was the best commentator
B) No one sifted this science like him
C) He wrote the most comprehensive book
D) He taught many students

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's mastery of kalam was so renowned that a contemporary scholar declared no one had 'sifted' the science like him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn something, do you desire that others see your expertise, or is your intention purely for Allah's sake? Reflect on Sanusi's satisfaction from seeing students learn, not from fame. In what area of your life are you seeking recognition rather than benefit?
?
Sanusi's teachers examined his works and extracted a book from them. Are you open to having your knowledge examined and critiqued by those more learned? Where do you fear correction?
?
Before leaving this world, Sanusi's eye was comforted by the spread of his works. What legacy of knowledge would you like to leave behind that continues to benefit others? What are you doing today to start building that legacy?

The Praise and Impact of Imam al-Sanusi
Author relates praise of Sanusi and describes the widespread study of his works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Sanusi's works, especially the Sanusiyyat, were received by scholars and students, and the lasting impact they had on Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
Sanusi's works were studied by teachers and students alike, ensuring his legacy.

This passage offers a window into the life and legacy of Imam al-Sanusi. We learn of the high praise he received from his contemporaries and the remarkable spread of his writings. This context helps us appreciate why his creedal works, particularly al-Sughra, became central texts in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words convey the historical context.
Notice the quote from Abu Imran and the description of Sanusi's joy.
2
Identify the key figures and works
Abu Imran praises Sanusi. Note that Sanusi's teachers also engaged with his works, extracting a book called 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. The Sanusiyyat include 'al-Sughra' and its commentary.
'Sanusiyyat' refers to his collection of creedal works.
3
Reflect on the impact described
The passage emphasises the extraordinary scholarly attention the Sanusiyyat received, with many commentaries and glosses. Ask yourself: why would a single creedal work receive such focus?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبو عمران موسى بن عقدة الأغضاوي إذا ذكر علم الكلام : ( ما رأيتُ من غربلَ هذا العلم مثل هذا الرجل ) يعني : السنوسي. وقبل رحيل إمامنا السنوسي عن هذه الدنيا الفانية . . قرَّت عينه حينما رأى مؤلفاته - وعلى رأسها « سنوسياته » ـ قد أقبل عليها طلبة العلم ، بل إنَّ بعض شيوخه نظر فيها واستخرج ما سُمِّي بكتاب حقائق السنوسي. أما الناظر في كتب فهارس المخطوطات فسيرى الأثر الكبير الذي أحدثته « السنوسيات » ؛ إذ أعملت العلماء أقلامها في شرح متونها ، والتحشية على شروحها ، ولا سيما « الصغرى » و « شرحها » ، فقد كانت لهذه العقيدة تحديداً عناية فائقة ندر أن ترى مثلها لكتاب في رحاب المكتبة الإسلامية.
Abu Imran Musa ibn Uqda al-Aghdawi, when mentioning the science of kalam, said: 'I have not seen anyone who sifted this science like this man' meaning al-Sanusi. Before our Imam al-Sanusi departed from this ephemeral world, his eye was comforted when he saw his works – foremost among them his 'Sanusiyyat' – being eagerly received by students of knowledge. Indeed, some of his teachers examined them and extracted what was called the book 'Haqa'iq al-Sanusi'. As for the one who looks into manuscript catalogues, he will see the great impact made by the 'Sanusiyyat'; for scholars employed their pens in commenting on its texts and in writing glosses upon its commentaries, especially 'al-Sughra' and its commentary. Indeed, this particular creed received extraordinary attention rarely seen for a book in the vastness of the Islamic library.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a biographical and historical introduction. It begins with a quote from a contemporary of Sanusi, Abu Imran Musa ibn Uqda al-Aghdawi, who praises Sanusi as the foremost scholar in the field of kalam ('the one who sifted this science'). Then it describes Sanusi's joy before his death at seeing his works (especially the 'Sanusiyyat') studied by students, and even his teachers extracting a book from them. Finally, it highlights the immense scholarly impact of the Sanusiyyat, noting that many scholars wrote commentaries and glosses on them, particularly on 'al-Sughra' and its commentary, and that this attention is unparalleled in Islamic literature.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The praise for Sanusi came only from his students.
Correction: The text shows that even established teachers studied his works and extracted a book from them, indicating deep scholarly respect.

Key Takeaway

Dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab Strengthens Sanusi
Narrates the dream that strengthened Sanusi against critics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab alleviated Sanusi's distress and silenced his critics, and how this episode illustrates the role of visionary dreams as divine confirmation in Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Divine confirmation can come through dreams, strengthening the believer against opposition.

Before we delve into Sanusi's theological arguments, it is helpful to know how he faced opposition. This biographical episode shows the human side of the scholar—his distress at criticism and the remarkable dream that turned the tide. Let's read the account of al-Hadiki.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the narrative flow.
Notice the emotional language: 'fire of jealousy', 'distressed', 'threatening'.
2
Trace the emotional arc
Identify four stages: the jealousy of critics, Sanusi's distress, the dream of Umar, and the transformation of both Sanusi and his critics.
What does it mean that Umar threatened him for 'fearing people'?
3
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this story illustrates the role of dreams in Islamic tradition—not as proof of orthodoxy but as personal divine comfort.
This relates to the common misunderstanding that dreams are definitive evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد اشتعلت نار الغيرة والحسد في قلوب بعض علماء عصره حينما رأوا عقائد السنوسي يُكتب لها التوفيق والمسيرة التعليمية ولم تلبث أن خبت ؛ رحمةً بالإمام وبهم من قبل ، قال العلامة المؤرخ الحضيكي في طبقاته : ( ولما ألف بعض عقائده أنكره كثير من علماء وقته ، وتكلموا بما لا يليق ، فكثر تغيره بذلك ، ثم رأى في منامه عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه واقفاً على رأسه يتهدده على الخوف من الناس ، فأصبح وقد زال حزنه ، وقوي على المنكرين فخرسوا من حينئذ ، ثم أقروا بفضله )
The fire of jealousy and envy ignited in the hearts of some scholars of his time when they saw Sanusi's creeds being granted success and educational progress, but it soon subsided; out of mercy for the Imam and for them before. The historian and scholar al-Hadiki said in his Tabaqat: 'When he authored some of his creeds, many scholars of his time denied them and spoke improperly. He became very distressed by that. Then he saw in his dream ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, standing at his head threatening him for fearing people. He awoke, his sadness had left him, and he became strong against the deniers, so they were silenced from then on, and then they acknowledged his merit.'
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage recounts an episode of opposition to Sanusi's creedal works. Some scholars, driven by jealousy and envy, criticized his writings. Sanusi became distressed. Then he saw in a dream ‘Umar ibn al-Khattab, who threatened him for fearing people rather than fearing God. After that dream, Sanusi's sadness disappeared, he gained strength, and his critics were silenced and eventually acknowledged his merit. The author notes that the fire of jealousy subsided as a mercy for both Sanusi and the critics themselves.

Key Terms

تغيره
رؤيا عمر بن الخطاب
المنكرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that dreams like the one Sanusi had serve as definitive proof of the truth of one's teachings or as a basis for theological claims.
Correction: In Islamic tradition, such dreams are considered personal divine comfort and encouragement, not public proofs of orthodoxy. The passage itself presents the dream as a source of strength for Sanusi, not as an argument for the correctness of his creed.

Key Takeaway
A personal dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab relieved Sanusi's distress and silenced his critics, showing that divine reassurance can come through dreams, though such dreams are not public proofs.

Opposition and Divine Encouragement in Sanusi's Life
Narrates the opposition Sanusi faced and the dream of Umar that reassured him.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Opposition to truth is temporary; divine support strengthens the believer.

This passage from Sanusi's biography shows that even great scholars face opposition. When his creeds were initially rejected, Sanusi felt distressed. But a dream of Umar ibn al-Khattab gave him courage. Let's read the account.

After this dream, Sanusi's sadness left him, he became strong against the critics, and the critics fell silent and eventually acknowledged his merit.

Spread of Sanusi's Creeds and Dreams of Endorsement
Describes the widespread acceptance of Sanusi's works and dreams confirming their merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's creeds gained widespread acceptance, including dream endorsements, and the critical scholarly reaction to their dominance.

Core Idea
Sanusi's creeds became so revered that even dreams confirmed their divine approval.

Sanusi's theological works, especially his Minor Creed, achieved extraordinary fame. Teachers and scholars carried them constantly, and dreams were reported where prophets taught them in Paradise. However, their dominance also sparked some scholarly annoyance. This passage from the biographical tradition captures both the reverence and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the narrative flow before analyzing details.
2
Identify elements of acceptance
Note the phrases indicating widespread acclaim, the story of al-Waryajili carrying the Minor Creed, and the dream of Ibrahim teaching it.
The dream is a 'glad tiding' endorsing the creed.
3
Note the critical perspective
Some scholars were annoyed that other creeds were dismissed for merely differing from Sanusi's, despite his works' merits.
This nuance balances the narrative.
4
Reflect on the role of dreams
Consider why dreams are mentioned as validation in Islamic tradition and what they add to the authority of a text.
5
Connect to the lesson objective
Summarize how the passage shows both the spread and the limits of Sanusi's influence, fulfilling the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خصوصاً وصارت تأليفه في علم التوحيد يُشار إليها بالبنان ، ووسمت بالفضل ، حتى قيل فيها : ( وعقائده كـافيـة فيه ، الصغرى » ، لا يعادلها شيء من العقائد كما أشار إليه ((۱) وكان الشيخ أبو محمد عبد الله الورياجلي قد نذر على نفسه ألا تفارقه « عقيدة السنوسي الصغرى » ، وقد جعلها في جيبه على جلالة قدره وعظيم إنصافه(٢) ومن جملة المبشِّرات التي رئيت : ما حكاه تلميذ الإمام السنوسي الشيخ الملالي في ( المواهب القدسية ) حيث قال : ( حدثني بعضهم أنه مات قريبه ، وكان صالحاً ، فرآه في النوم فسأله عن حاله ، فقال : دخلت الجنة ، فرأيت إبراهيم الخليل عليه السلام يُقرئ صبياناً عقيدة السنوسي » ، يدرسونها في الألواح يجهرون بقراءتها ((۳). وقد بلغ شأن « السنوسيات » عموماً و « شرح الصغرى » خصوصاً شأواً جعل بعض محققي العلماء يتضجر من ذلك لا من باب الاعتراض على ما حوته من علم جم ، بل أن يُخطَّأ ما سواها لمجرد مخالفتها ، حتى قال العلامة الشيخ حسن العطار في « حاشيته على شرح المحلي لجمع الجوامع » : ( ليت هذا القائل عاش حتى الآن ؛ ليرى ما يقوله المدرسون في دروسهم، بل ما ينقله المؤلفون في
Especially, his writings in the science of tawhid became pointed to with fingers and were characterized by excellence, to the point that it was said about them: 'His creeds are sufficient in it, the Minor (creed) is not equaled by any other creed' as was indicated. And Shaykh Abu Muhammad 'Abd Allah al-Waryajili had vowed to himself that the Minor Creed of al-Sanusi would never leave him, and he kept it in his pocket despite his high status and great impartiality. Among the glad tidings that were seen: what the student of Imam al-Sanusi, Shaykh al-Mallali, narrated in al-Mawahib al-Qudsiyya: 'Some told me that a relative of his died, and he was righteous. He saw him in a dream and asked about his state. He said: I entered Paradise and saw Ibrahim al-Khalil, peace be upon him, teaching children the creed of al-Sanusi; they were studying it on tablets, reciting it aloud.' And the status of 'al-Sanusiyyat' in general and 'Sharh al-Sughra' in particular reached such a level that it caused some discerning scholars to become annoyed—not out of objection to the vast knowledge they contain, but because other creeds were deemed mistaken simply for differing from them. Until the scholar Shaykh Hasan al-‘Attar said in his gloss on Sharh al-Mahalli on Jam‘ al-Jawami‘: 'Would that this speaker had lived until now to see what teachers say in their lessons, rather what authors transmit in...'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage describes the extraordinary reception of Sanusi's theological works. His writings are acclaimed as unparalleled, and the Minor Creed was said to be sufficient and matchless. The scholar al-Waryajili carried it in his pocket at all times. Further confirmation came in a dream where Prophet Ibrahim is seen teaching Sanusi's creed to children in Paradise. However, the success of these works led some scholars to be annoyed that other creeds were criticized solely for disagreeing with Sanusi's views, a point noted by Hasan al-‘Attar.

The Author's Own Naming of the Creeds
Sanusi's own designation of his creeds and justification for the common names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Sanusi himself named his creedal works and why the common names (Kubra, Wusta, Sughra) are justified.

Core Idea
Sanusi himself used the names Kubra, Wusta, Sughra — they are not later inventions.

This passage addresses a common question about the naming of Sanusi's creeds. The editor clarifies that while Sanusi originally called his creed 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' and its commentary 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid', he himself referred to them as al-Kubra, al-Wusta, and al-Sughra in his own writings. This lesson examines the evidence and explains why using these names is acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the official names and the evidence Sanusi gives.
Pay attention to the phrase 'عقيدتنا الكبرى' in the Arabic.
2
Identify the key evidence
Find the quote from Sanusi's commentary on al-Aqida al-Wusta where he refers to 'our Aqida al-Kubra'. This shows he used the name al-Kubra himself.
This quote is provided in parentheses in the passage.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: Some might think the names Kubra, Wusta, Sughra are later additions, but the passage proves they were used by the author himself, so there is no blame in using them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نصَّ إمامنا السنوسي : أنَّ عقيدته هذه اسمها : « عقيدة أهل التوحيد » ، وأنَّ شرحها الذي بين أيدينا اسمه : « عمدة أهل التوفيق والتسديد » ، وما زاد فإنما هو من قبيل الأوصاف ، كما نبه على ذلك العلامة العكاري وبعده العلامة الحامدي ويظهر أن المؤلف نفسَهُ كان يسمِّي عقائده بما درجت عليه الألسنة اليوم ؛ من « كبرى » و « وسطیٰ » و «صغری» و «صغری الصغرى » ، فلا ضير إذاً من إطلاق هذا في حقها ؛ فقد قال في شرح العقيدة الوسطى » محيلاً على شرح العقيدة الكبرى » للتوسع الاستدلال : ( وأما السابع - وهو إثبات حوادث لا أول لها ـ فله أدلة كثيرة ، ذكرناها في ( عقيدتنا الكبرى » و « شرحها » ) (۱) ، فلا تثريب على من درج على هذه الأعلام لعقائد الإمام السنوسي وقد اعتنى كبار العلماء بـ ( شرح العقيدة الكبرى » ، يعرفك على هذا النسخ الخطية التي اعتمدت في إخراج هذه الطبعة التي بين يديك ، والتي كتبتها أقلام أعلام من علماء هذا الفن وأقطابه ، وعلى رأسهم العلامة المنجور صاحب الحاشية على شرح العقيدة
Our Imam al-Sanusi stated that his creed is named 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid', and that its commentary before us is named 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid', and anything additional is only from the category of descriptions, as pointed out by al-Allama al-Akkari and after him al-Allama al-Hamidi. And it appears that the author himself used to name his creeds by what the tongues have become accustomed to today: 'Kubra', 'Wusta', 'Sughra', and 'Sughra al-Sughra'. So there is no harm in applying this to them; for he said in his commentary on al-Aqida al-Wusta, referring to the commentary on al-Aqida al-Kubra for extensiveness of argumentation: 'As for the seventh—which is the affirmation of events without beginning—it has many proofs, which we have mentioned in our Aqida al-Kubra and its commentary.' So there is no blame on one who follows these designations for the creeds of Imam al-Sanusi. And great scholars have paid attention to Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra, known to you through the manuscripts relied upon in producing this edition before you, written by the pens of prominent scholars of this art and its poles, headed by al-Allama al-Manjur, author of the gloss on Sharh al-Aqida.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage clarifies the naming of Imam al-Sanusi's creedal works. The editor quotes Sanusi himself stating that his creed is called 'Aqidat Ahl al-Tawhid' and its commentary 'Umdat Ahl al-Tawfiq wa al-Tasdid', and that additional names are merely descriptive. However, the editor notes that Sanusi himself used the now-common names: al-Kubra (the greater), al-Wusta (the middle), al-Sughra (the smaller), and Sughra al-Sughra (the smallest of the small). Evidence is given from Sanusi's commentary on al-Aqida al-Wusta, where he refers to 'our Aqida al-Kubra and its commentary' for further proofs on the topic of events without beginning. Therefore, there is no blame in using these titles. The passage also notes that major scholars, such as al-Allama al-Manjur (author of a gloss on Sharh al-Aqida), have paid attention to Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra, as evidenced by the manuscripts used in this edition.

Key Terms
عقيدة أهل التوحيد
عمدة أهل التوفيق والتسديد
الكبرى
الوسطى
الصغرى
صغرى الصغرى
العلامة المنجور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the names Kubra, Wusta, Sughra were later inventions by students or editors.
Correction: The passage shows that Sanusi himself used these names in his own commentary on al-Aqida al-Wusta, when he referred to 'our Aqida al-Kubra'. Thus, the names have authorial backing.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the editor provide that Sanusi himself used the names 'Kubra', 'Wusta', 'Sughra' for his creeds?
A) A statement from al-Akkari
B) A statement from al-Hamidi
C) A reference in Sharh al-Aqida al-Wusta to 'our Aqida al-Kubra'
D) A note from al-Manjur's gloss

Key Takeaway
Sanusi himself referred to his creeds as al-Kubra, al-Wusta, and al-Sughra, so these names are authentic and can be used without blame.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Sanusi was flexible about the names of his creeds, why are you rigid about the labels you attach to your own beliefs? Will you let go of sectarian names and focus on the truth they convey?
?
Do you know the content of the middle creed well enough to explain it without mentioning its name? Test yourself: Summarize the key points of al-Aqida al-Wusta from memory.

The 'Aqida al-Kubra: Sanusi's First Work
Notes scholarly reception and Sanusi's first work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of al-Sanusi's 'Aqida al-Kubra as his first theological work and the scholarly reception it received.

Core Idea
The 'Aqida al-Kubra was Sanusi's first composition in kalam, not the Sanusiyyat.

This passage highlights two important facts about al-Sanusi's 'Aqida al-Kubra: its widespread scholarly reception and its place as the author's first work in theology. It also corrects a common misunderstanding about the sequence of his compositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's own words inform your understanding before analysis.
Notice the two distinct points: scholars' attention and the first-work claim.
2

3

Many assume the Sanusiyyat were his earliest works. This passage corrects that: the 'Aqida al-Kubra came first. Consider why this matters for understanding Sanusi's intellectual development.

?
Scholars invested deeply in studying and glossing al-Sanusi's 'Aqida al-Kubra. Scholarly Rivalry Between Sanusi and Ibn Zakri
Describes the noble scholarly competition between al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri al-Tilimsani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the scholarly rivalry between al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri and how it reflects the respectful competition in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
Scholarly rivalry can be noble and respectful, like racehorses striving for truth.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary gives us a glimpse into the dynamics of classical Islamic scholarship. It describes the relationship between two great scholars, al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri, not as personal enmity but as a dignified competition in the pursuit of knowledge. Let's explore what this tells us about the etiquette of scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation to grasp the literal meaning. Note the key terms used.
Pay attention to the metaphor of racehorses.
2
Identify how al-Sanusi refers to Ibn Zakri
Notice that al-Sanusi calls him 'from the people of the age' rather than naming him directly. Ask yourself why.
This reflects scholarly etiquette.
3
Analyze the metaphor
The two scholars are compared to racehorses. Consider what this reveals about their relationship and status.
Racehorses imply equality and spirited competition.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The passage states that final precedence went to al-Sanusi. Think about what this means for their legacies.
Precedence (al-sabq) indicates lasting influence.
5
Summarize the lesson
Combine your observations into one sentence about the nature of scholarly rivalry in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما تظهر في هذا الكتاب شرح العقيدة الكبرى الخصومةُ العلمية الشريفة بين إمامنا السنوسي والعلامة المصقع ابن زكري التلمساني ، والإمام يكتفي عند ذكره ونقل أقواله بأنه من أهل العصر ، والإمامانِ يومها في هذا الفنّ كفرسَيْ رهان ، وإن كان السبق انتهاءً لصاحب ) السنوسيات » .
Likewise, in this book, Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra, appears the noble scholarly rivalry between our Imam al-Sanusi and the eloquent scholar Ibn Zakri al-Tilimsani. The Imam, when mentioning him and transmitting his statements, suffices with saying that he is from the people of the age. Both Imams at that time in this art were like two racehorses, although the final precedence went to the author of the Sanusiyyat.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage highlights the respectful scholarly competition between al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri. Al-Sanusi, when citing Ibn Zakri's views, merely refers to him as 'from the people of the age' (i.e., a contemporary scholar), without naming him directly. The two were equals in the field of kalam, likened to two racehorses. However, the final victory (al-sabq) belonged to al-Sanusi, the author of the Sanusiyyat.

Key Terms
الخصومة العلمية
فن
سبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the rivalry was hostile or personal.
Correction: The passage describes it as a 'noble scholarly rivalry' and uses the metaphor of racehorses, indicating respectful competition rather than enmity.

Checkpoint

C) As a heretic.
D) He does not mention him.

Key Takeaway
The scholarly rivalry between al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri exemplifies the noble pursuit of knowledge where scholars compete respectfully, with the ultimate precedence belonging to al-Sanusi's work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in intellectual or religious discussions with peers, do you view them as competitors to be defeated or as racehorses striving alongside you for truth? How does your attitude reflect the adab shown by Imam al-Sanusi? What would change if you adopted his respectful distance?
?
Have you ever had a scholarly disagreement where you felt the need to diminish the other person's status to elevate your own? How does Imam al-Sanusi's example of simply calling Ibn Zakri 'from the people of the age' challenge you to be more humble and fair?
?
The passage says the final precedence went to al-Sanusi. In your pursuit of knowledge, are you more concerned with being recognized as the one who 'wins' or with the truth itself? How can you ensure your efforts are for Allah and not for reputation?

Regional Reputations of Sanusi and Ibn Zakri
Al-Shafshawi compares the reputations of Sanusi and Ibn Zakri across two regions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different qualities that scholars of Tlemcen and the Maghreb emphasized in their assessments of al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri.

Core Idea
Scholars valued different strengths: Sanusi for verification and piety, Ibn Zakri for breadth of learning.

Al-Shafshawi provides a helpful snapshot of how two prominent scholars were regarded in different regions. This passage highlights that scholarly reputation is not monolithic; different communities may emphasize different virtues. As you read, notice the specific qualities attributed to each scholar and how they reflect the values of their respective circles.

Guided Path

Read the passage carefully

The passage is a quote from al-Shafshawi.

List the attributes mentioned for al-Sanusi and for Ibn Zakri. For Sanusi: tahqiq, wilaya, zuhd. For Ibn Zakri: tabahhur, riwaya, high rank in manqul and ma'qul.
Note that Tlemcen scholars magnify both but for different reasons.

قال العلامة الشفشاوني : ( وبالجملة : فعلماء تلمسان يذكرون الشيخ السنوسي ويعظمونه بالتحقيق والولاية والزهد في الدنيا ويعظمون الشيخ ابن زكري بتبحره في العلوم ، واتساعه في الرواية ، وعلو طبقاته في المنقول والمعقول ، ويقولون : هو علامة الوقت ، وأهل المغرب الأقصى يفضّلون السنوسي من جهة التحقيق

And the people of the far Maghreb prefer al-Sanusi in terms of verification.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage cites al-Shafshawi's assessment of the reputations of Sanusi and Ibn Zakri. According to him, the scholars of Tlemcen honor Sanusi for his verification, saintly status, and asceticism; they honor Ibn Zakri for his deep knowledge, extensive narration, and high standing in both transmitted and rational sciences. They call Ibn Zakri the scholar of the time. Meanwhile, the people of the far Maghreb (Morocco) prefer Sanusi in the area of verification.

Key Takeaway
The scholars of Tlemcen and the Maghreb valued different aspects of Sanusi and Ibn Zakri's scholarship, with Sanusi recognized for verification and piety, and Ibn Zakri for breadth of learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Tlemcen scholars magnified both Sanusi and Ibn Zakri for different strengths. Do you have the humility to recognize excellence in others that differs from your own strengths? Or do you only value what you already have?

Sanusi's Severe Judgment on the Muqallid
Editor notes Ibn Dahaq's influence on Sanusi's view of taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Sanusi, influenced by Ibn Dahaq, held that the muqallid is not a believer in God's sight yet is still considered a believer in this world due to uttering the shahada.

Core Idea
Sanusi's view on taqlid: harsh in the afterlife, but merciful in this world.

Imam al-Sanusi's position on the spiritual status of those who blindly follow religious beliefs without independent reasoning is surprisingly severe. However, as this passage reveals, his judgment applies only to the afterlife; in worldly terms, such a person remains part of the Muslim community. This distinction between worldly and otherworldly status is crucial for understanding later kalam discussions on faith and the place of the muqallid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the footnote marker (1) and how it qualifies the main statement.
The passage is a historical note from the editor, not Sanusi's own words.
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms 'taqlid', 'muqallid', 'iman', and 'shahadatan' in the Arabic. Understand their basic meanings in this context.
These terms are central to the discussion of faith and authority.
3
Examine the footnote clarification
The footnote explains that Sanusi's judgment ('not a believer') refers only to God's sight (the afterlife). In this world, the muqallid is considered a believer due to having uttered the shahada.
This duality prevents misunderstanding Sanusi's position as excommunication.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why Sanusi might adopt such a strong position on taqlid. How does this distinction between worldly and otherworldly status affect the concept of faith in kalam?
This connects to broader debates about the necessity of proof (dalil) for true iman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويظهر بجلاء تأثير العلامة ابن دهاق في إمامنا السنوسي في التقليد والحكم على المقلد، فهو هنا قد أخذ بأشد الأقوال؛ إذ قال بعدم إيمان المقلد (١) ، وشهد لذلك بمقطَّعات نقلها عن العلامة ابن دهاق. (١) يعني: عند الله تعالى، أما في الدنيا فهو مؤمن؛ لحرمة النطق بالشهادتين
The influence of the scholar Ibn Dahaq on our Imam al-Sanusi regarding taqlid and the judgment on the muqallid appears clearly, for he here adopted the strongest of the opinions; he said that the muqallid is not a believer (1), and he testified to that with excerpts he transmitted from the scholar Ibn Dahaq. (1) Meaning: in the sight of God Almighty, but in this world he is a believer because of the sanctity of uttering the two testimonies.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a historical note by the editor about a doctrinal stance of Imam al-Sanusi. It points out that Sanusi was influenced by Ibn Dahaq regarding the issue of taqlid (imitative belief) and the judgment on the muqallid (one who believes based on authority without independent reasoning). Sanusi reportedly held the strictest view: that the muqallid is not a believer. However, the editor adds a clarification in a footnote: this is only in the sight of God (i.e., in the afterlife); in this world, the muqallid is considered a believer because of the inviolability of having uttered the shahada. Thus, the worldly judgment remains that of Islam, but the ultimate spiritual status is severe.

Key Terms
تقليد
مقلد
إيمان
شهادتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi declared muqallids to be non-Muslims in this world as well.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Sanusi's statement that the muqallid is 'not a believer' applies only in the sight of God (the afterlife). In this world, the muqallid is considered a believer because of the sanctity of uttering the two testimonies.

Checkpoint

A) Believer in this world and the afterlife
B) Not a believer in God's sight, but considered a believer in this world
C) A disbeliever in both worlds
D) A believer only if he performs independent reasoning

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's severe judgment on the muqallid is limited to God's sight; in this world, the muqallid remains a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If your faith were judged by your level of certainty rather than just your shahada, would you be confident in your īmān? What step can you take today to move from taqlid to tahqeeq?
?
Do you treat people who only have an outward shahada as true believers in your heart, or do you judge them inwardly? What does Sanusi's mercy in this world teach you about your own attitude?

Scholarly Rivalry Between Sanusi and Ibn Zakri
Editor describes respectful scholarly rivalry between Sanusi and Ibn Zakri.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble scholarly rivalry between Imam al-Sanusi and Ibn Zakri and how their contemporaries valued different aspects of their scholarship.

Core Idea
Scholarly rivalry can be noble, respectful, and complementary, not hostile.

This lesson provides a fascinating glimpse into the scholarly world of 9th/15th century North Africa. The editor of Sanusi's commentary presents the relationship between two great scholars—Sanusi and Ibn Zakri—as a respectful competition. Their contemporaries recognized different strengths in each, showing that intellectual excellence can take different forms. The passage also includes a balanced assessment from al-Shafshawani that highlights the virtues of both scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the tone: respectful and admiring of both scholars.
Pay attention to the term 'kifarsi rihan' (like two racehorses).
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the key terms: خصومة علمية (scholarly rivalry), علامة مصقع (eloquent scholar), فن (art/science), كفرسي رهان (like two racehorses), تحقيق (verification), انقطاع (devotion). Understand their meanings.
These terms show the high regard for both scholars.
3
Analyze al-Shafshawani's quote
The quote contrasts how Tlemcen scholars and far Maghreb people view the two. Note the specific strengths attributed to each Sanusi: tahqiq and inqita'; Ibn Zakri: extensive knowledge and transmission.
This shows a nuanced, respectful comparison.
4

Consider what this passage teaches about how scholars should interact. The rivalry is 'sharifa' (noble) and the editor gives a balanced view.
This is a model for respectful academic discourse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما تظهر في هذا الكتاب "شرح العقيدة الكبرى" الخصومةُ العلمية الشريفة بين إمامنا السنوسي والعلامة المصقع ابن زكري التلمساني، والإمام يكتفي عند ذكره ونقل أقواله بأنه من أهل العصر، والإمامانِ يومها في هذا الفنّ كفرسَيْ رهان، وإن كان السبق انتهاءً لصاحب "السنوسيات". قال العلامة الشفشاوني: (وبالجملة: فعلماء تلمسان يذكرون الشيخ السنوسي ويعظمونه بالتحقيق والولاية والزهد في الدنيا ويعظمون الشيخ ابن زكري بتبحره في العلوم، واتساعه في الرواية، وعلو طبقاته في المنقول والمعقول، ويقولون: هو علامة الوقت، وأهل المغرب الأقصى يفضّلون السنوسي من جهة التحقيق، والانقطاع إلى الله تعالى، وابن زكري كان له الصيت البعيد والجاه العظيم عند الملوك وغيرهم، رحمهما الله تعالى).

Notable Commentators on Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra
The editor lists notable commentators on Sanusi's work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key commentators on Sanusi's Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra and the significance of their glosses.

Core Idea
The commentary tradition shapes how we understand Sanusi's text.

This lesson introduces the major commentators who wrote glosses on Sanusi's Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra. Knowing these scholars helps us appreciate the living tradition of Islamic scholarship and the ongoing engagement with this foundational text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the list of commentators.
Pay attention to the names and any special notes about each scholar.
2
Identify the commentators
List each commentator mentioned and note any distinguishing details, such as who wrote the earliest gloss or who taught at al-Qarawiyyin.
3
Understand the context
Reflect on why these glosses were written and how they contribute to the transmission of Sanusi's work.
Consider the chain of scholarly commentary.
4
Consider the significance
Note the editor's remark that al-Hamidi's gloss is among the finest and that he frequently quotes al-Akkari. Think about what this says about scholarly reliance and tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولـ"شرح العقيدة الكبرى" حواش عديدة سطرتها أعلام العلماء، ولعل من أقدم حواشيها "حاشية العلامة المنجور المكناسي"، ومنهم: العلامة الفقيه المالكي علي بن أحمد الجزولي الرسموكي، والعلامة المحقق أبو علي الحسن اليوسي، والشيخ علي العكاري المراكشي، وكان قد درسها وشرحها بالقرويين، والشيخ المحقق محمد عليش المالكي، ومن المتأخرين العلامة المحقق إسماعيل الحامدي، وحواشيه عليها من أنفس ما كُتب، وهو كثير النقل عن العلامة العكاري، رحمهم الله جميعاً.
And 'Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra' has numerous glosses penned by leading scholars. Perhaps among the earliest of its glosses is 'Hashiyat al-Allama al-Manjur al-Maknasi.' Among them: the scholar and Maliki faqih Ali ibn Ahmad al-Jazuli al-Rasmuki, the verifier Abu Ali al-Hasan al-Yusi, Shaykh Ali al-Akkari al-Marrakshi (who had studied and explained it at al-Qarawiyyin), the verifier Muhammad Ulysh al-Maliki, and among the later ones, the verifier Ismail al-Hamidi. His glosses on it are among the finest written, and he frequently quotes from al-Akkari. May God have mercy on them all.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an editorial note listing the major commentators (haashiya authors) on 'Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra'. The editor states that there are many glosses, and he names several: al-Manjur al-Maknasi (possibly the earliest), Ali ibn Ahmad al-Jazuli, Abu Ali al-Hasan al-Yusi, Ali al-Akkari (who also taught the work at al-Qarawiyyin), Muhammad Ulysh, and the later Ismail al-Hamidi. The editor notes that al-Hamidi's gloss is especially valuable and that he relies heavily on al-Akkari. The passage concludes with a prayer for mercy on all these scholars.

Key Terms

المنجور المكناسي
العكاري
القرويين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the earliest gloss is the most important, but the editor does not rank them by importance.
Correction: The editor merely notes al-Manjur al-Maknasi's gloss as possibly the earliest, but praises al-Hamidi's gloss as among the finest, indicating that importance is not tied to chronology alone.

Which commentator is described as having studied and explained Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra at al-Qarawiyyin?

D) Muhammad Ulysh al-Maliki

Key Takeaway
The glosses on Sharh al-'Aqidah al-Kubra represent a rich tradition of scholarly commentary, with al-Hamidi's work being particularly notable for its reliance on earlier authorities like al-Akkari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study religious texts, do you actively seek out the chain of scholars who transmitted and explained them? How can you cultivate gratitude for their efforts in your daily reading?
?
Al-Hamidi's gloss is praised for building upon al-Akkari's work. Are you humble enough to learn from and credit those who came before you, rather than seeking originality for its own sake?
?
This lesson shows a tradition of scholarly commentary. If you were to write a reflection on a text you study, what would it reveal about your understanding and your connection to the scholarly tradition?
